Introduction
Model theory is a branch of mathematical logic where we study mathemat-
ical structures by considering the ï¬rst-order sentences true in those struc-
tures and the sets deï¬nable by ï¬rst-order formulas. Traditionally there have
been two principal themes in the subject:
â€¢ starting with a concrete mathematical structure, such as the ï¬eld of real
numbers, and using model-theoretic techniques to obtain new information
about the structure and the sets deï¬nable in the structure;
â€¢ looking at theories that have some interesting property and proving
general structure theorems about their models.
A good example of the ï¬rst theme is Tarskiâ€™s work on the ï¬eld of real
numbers. Tarski showed that the theory of the real ï¬eld is decidable. This
is a sharp contrast to GÂ¨odelâ€™s Incompleteness Theorem, which showed that
the theory of the seemingly simpler ring of integers is undecidable. For his
proof, Tarski developed the method of quantiï¬er elimination which can be
used to show that all subsets of Rn deï¬nable in the real ï¬eld are geomet-
rically well-behaved. More recently, Wilkie [103] extended these ideas to
prove that sets deï¬nable in the real exponential ï¬eld are also well-behaved.
The second theme is illustrated by Morleyâ€™s Categoricity Theorem, which
says that if T is a theory in a countable language and there is an uncount-
able cardinal Îº such that, up to isomorphism, T has a unique model of
cardinality Îº, then T has a unique model of cardinality Î» for every un-
countable Îº. This line has been extended by Shelah [92], who has developed
deep general classiï¬cation results.
For some time, these two themes seemed like opposing directions in the
subject, but over the last decade or so we have come to realize that there

2
Introduction
are fascinating connections between these two lines. Classical mathematical
structures, such as groups and ï¬elds, arise in surprising ways when we study
general classiï¬cation problems, and ideas developed in abstract settings
have surprising applications to concrete mathematical structures. The most
striking example of this synthesis is Hrushovskiâ€™s [43] application of very
general model-theoretic methods to prove the Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture for
function ï¬elds.
My goal was to write an introductory text in model theory that, in ad-
dition to developing the basic material, illustrates the abstract and applied
directions of the subject and the interaction of these two programs.
Chapter 1 begins with the basic deï¬nitions and examples of languages,
structures, and theories. Most of this chapter is routine, but, because study-
ing deï¬nability and interpretability is one of the main themes of the subject,
I have included some nontrivial examples. Section 1.3 ends with a quick in-
troduction to Meq. This is a rather technical idea that will not be needed
until Chapter 6 and can be omitted on ï¬rst reading.
The ï¬rst results of the subject, the Compactness Theorem and the
LÂ¨owenheimâ€“Skolem Theorem, are introduced in Chapter 2. In Section 2.2
we show that even these basic results have interesting mathematical con-
sequences by proving the decidability of the theory of the complex ï¬eld.
Section 2.4 discusses the back-and-forth method beginning with Cantorâ€™s
analysis of countable dense linear orders and moving on to Ehrenfeuchtâ€“
FraÂ¨Ä±ssÂ´e Games and Scottâ€™s result that countable structures are determined
up to isomorphism by a single inï¬nitary sentence.
Chapter 3 shows how the ideas from Chapter 2 can be used to develop
a model-theoretic test for quantiï¬er elimination. We then prove quantiï¬er
elimination for the ï¬elds of real and complex numbers and use these results
to study deï¬nable sets.
Chapters 4 and 5 are devoted to the main model-building tools of clas-
sical model theory. We begin by introducing types and then study struc-
tures built by either realizing or omitting types. In particular, we study
prime, saturated, and homogeneous models. In Section 4.3, we show that
even these abstract constructions have algebraic applications by giving a
new quantiï¬er elimination criterion and applying it to diï¬€erentially closed
ï¬elds. The methods of Sections 4.2 and 4.3 are used to study countable
models in Section 4.4, where we examine â„µ0-categorical theories and prove
Morleyâ€™s result on the number of countable models. The ï¬rst two sections
of Chapter 5 are devoted to basic results on indiscernibles. We then illus-
trate the usefulness of indiscernibles with two important applicationsâ€”a
special case of Shelahâ€™s Many-Models Theorem in Section 5.3 and the Parisâ€“
Harrington independence result in Section 5.4. Indiscernibles also later play
an important role in Section 6.5.
Chapter 6 begins with a proof of Morleyâ€™s Categoricity Theorem in the
spirit of Baldwin and Lachlan. The Categoricity Theorem can be thought
of as the beginning of modern model theory and the rest of the book is

Introduction
3
devoted to giving the ï¬‚avor of the subject. I have made a conscious ped-
agogical choice to focus on Ï‰-stable theories and avoid the generality of
stability, superstability, or simplicity. In this context, forking has a con-
crete explanation in terms of Morley rank. One can quickly develop some
general tools and then move on to see their applications. Sections 6.2 and
6.3 are rather technical developments of the machinery of Morley rank and
the basic results on forking and independence. These ideas are applied in
Sections 6.4 and 6.5 to study prime model extensions and saturated models
of Ï‰-stable theories.
Chapters 7 and 8 are intended to give a quick but, I hope, seductive
glimpse at some current directions in the subject. It is often interesting
to study algebraic objects with additional model-theoretic hypotheses. In
Chapter 7 we study Ï‰-stable groups and show that they share many prop-
erties with algebraic groups over algebraically closed ï¬elds. We also include
Hrushovskiâ€™s theorem about recovering a group from a generically associa-
tive operation which is a generalization of Weilâ€™s theorem on group chunks.
Chapter 8 begins with a seemingly abstract discussion of the combinatorial
geometry of algebraic closure on strongly minimal sets, but we see in Sec-
tion 8.3 that this geometry has a great deal of inï¬‚uence on what algebraic
objects are interpretable in a structure. We conclude with an outline of
Hrushovskiâ€™s proof of the Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture in one special case.
Because I was trying to write an introductory text rather than an en-
cyclopedic treatment, I have had to make a number of ruthless decisions
about what to include and what to omit. Some interesting topics, such as
ultraproducts, recursive saturation, and models of arithmetic, are relegated
to the exercises. Others, such as modules, the p-adic ï¬eld, or ï¬nite model
theory, are omitted entirely. I have also frequently chosen to present the-
orems in special cases when, in fact, we know much more general results.
Not everyone would agree with these choices.
The Reader
While writing this book I had in mind three types of readers:
â€¢ graduate students considering doing research in model theory;
â€¢ graduate students in logic outside of model theory;
â€¢ mathematicians in areas outside of logic where model theory has had
interesting applications.
For the graduate student in model theory, this book should provide a ï¬rm
foundation in the basic results of the subject while whetting the appetite
for further exploration. My hope is that the applications given in Chapters
7 and 8 will excite students and lead them to read the advanced texts [7],
[18], [76], and [86] written by my friends.
The graduate student in logic outside of model theory should, in addition
to learning the basics, get an idea of some of the main directions of the
modern subject. I have also included a number of special topics that I

think every logician should see at some point, namely the random graph,
Ehrenfeuchtâ€“FraÂ¨Ä±ssÂ´e Games, Scottâ€™s Isomorphism Theorem, Morleyâ€™s result
on the number of countable models, Shelahâ€™s Many-Models Theorem, and
the Parisâ€“Harrington Theorem.
For the mathematician interested in applications, I have tried to illus-
trate several of the ways that model theory can be a useful tool in analyzing
classical mathematical structures. In Chapter 3, we develop the method of
quantiï¬er elimination and show how it can be used to prove results about
algebraically closed ï¬elds and real closed ï¬elds. One of the areas where
model-theoretic ideas have had the most fruitful impact is diï¬€erential al-
gebra. In Chapter 4, we introduce diï¬€erentially closed ï¬elds. Diï¬€erentially
closed ï¬elds are very interesting Ï‰-stable structures. Chapters 6, 7, and 8
contain a number of illustrations of the impact of stability-theoretic ideas
on diï¬€erential algebra. In particular, in Section 7.4 we give Poizatâ€™s proof of
Kolchinâ€™s theorem on diï¬€erential Galois groups of strongly normal exten-
sions. In Chapter 7, we look at classical mathematical objectsâ€”groupsâ€”
under additional model-theoretic assumptionsâ€”Ï‰-stability. We also use
these ideas to give more information about algebraically closed ï¬elds. In
Section 8.3, we give an idea of how ideas from geometric model theory can
be used to answer questions in Diophantine geometry.
Prerequisites
Chapter 1 begins with the basic deï¬nitions of languages and structures.
Although a mathematically sophisticated reader with little background in
mathematical logic should be able to read this book, I expect that most
readers will have seen this material before. The ideal reader will have
already taken one graduate or undergraduate course in logic and be ac-
quainted with mathematical structures, formal proofs, GÂ¨odelâ€™s Complete-
ness and Incompleteness Theorems, and the basics about computability.
Shoenï¬eldâ€™s Mathematical Logic [94] or Ebbinghaus, Flum, and Thomasâ€™
Mathematical Logic [31] are good references.
I will assume that the reader has some familiarity with very basic set
theory, including Zornâ€™s Lemma, ordinals, and cardinals. Appendix A sum-
marizes all of this material. More sophisticated ideas from combinatorial
set theory are needed in Chapter 5 but are developed completely in the
text.
Many of the applications and examples that we will investigate come from
algebra. The ideal reader will have had a year-long graduate algebra course
and be comfortable with the basics about groups, commutative rings, and
ï¬elds. Because I suspect that many readers will not have encountered the
algebra of formally real ï¬elds that is essential in Section 3.3, I have included
this material in Appendix B. Langâ€™s Algebra [58] is a good reference for most
of the material we will need. Ideally the reader will have also seen some
elementary algebraic geometry, but we introduce this material as needed.

Using This Book as a Text
I suspect that in most courses where this book is used as a text, the students
will have already seen most of the material in Sections 1.1, 1.2, and 2.1. A
reasonable one-semester course would cover Sections 2.2, 2.3, the beginning
of 2.4, 3.1, 3.2, 4.1â€“4.3, the beginning of 4.4, 5.1, 5.2, and 6.1. In a year-
long course, one has the luxury of picking and choosing extra topics from
the remaining text. My own choices would certainly include Sections 3.3,
6.2â€“6.4, 7.1, and 7.2.
Exercises and Remarks
Each chapter ends with a section of exercises and remarks. The exercises
range from quite easy to quite challenging. Some of the exercises develop
important ideas that I would have included in a longer text. I have left
some important results as exercises because I think students will beneï¬t
by working them out. Occasionally, I refer to a result or example from the
exercises later in the text. Some exercises will require more comfort with
algebra, computability, or set theory than I assume in the rest of the book.
I mark those exercises with a dagger.â€ 
The Remarks sections have two purposes. I make some historical remarks
and attributions. With a few exceptions, I tend to give references to sec-
ondary sources with good presentations rather than the original source. I
also use the Remarks section to describe further results and give suggestions
for further reading.

of B, and A âŠ‚B means A is a proper subset (i.e., A âŠ†B but A Ì¸= B).

Structures and Theories
1.1
Languages and Structures
In mathematical logic, we use ï¬rst-order languages to describe mathe-
matical structures. Intuitively, a structure is a set that we wish to study
equipped with a collection of distinguished functions, relations, and ele-
ments. We then choose a language where we can talk about the distin-
guished functions, relations, and elements and nothing more. For example,
when we study the ordered ï¬eld of real numbers with the exponential func-
tion, we study the structure (R, +, Â·, exp, <, 0, 1), where the underlying set
is the set of real numbers, and we distinguish the binary functions addition
and multiplication, the unary function x 	â†’ex, the binary order relation,
and the real numbers 0 and 1. To describe this structure, we would use a lan-
guage where we have symbols for +, Â·, exp, <, 0, 1 and can write statements
such as âˆ€xâˆ€y exp(x)Â·exp(y) = exp(x+y) and âˆ€x (x > 0 â†’âˆƒy exp(y) = x).
We interpret these statements as the assertions â€œexey = ex+y for all x and
yâ€ and â€œfor all positive x, there is a y such that ey = x.â€
For another example, we might consider the structure (N, +, 0, 1) of the
natural numbers with addition and distinguished elements 0 and 1. The
natural language for studying this structure is the language where we have
a binary function symbol for addition and constant symbols for 0 and 1.
We would write sentences such as âˆ€xâˆƒy (x = y +y âˆ¨x = y +y +1), which
we interpret as the assertion that â€œevery number is either even or 1 plus
an even number.â€

8
1. Structures and Theories
Deï¬nition 1.1.1 A language L is given by specifying the following data:
i) a set of function symbols F and positive integers nf for each f âˆˆF;
ii) a set of relation symbols R and positive integers nR for each R âˆˆR;
iii) a set of constant symbols C.
The numbers nf and nR tell us that f is a function of nf variables and
R is an nR-ary relation.
Any or all of the sets F, R, and C may be empty. Examples of languages
include:
i) the language of rings Lr = {+, âˆ’, Â·, 0, 1}, where +, âˆ’and Â· are binary
function symbols and 0 and 1 are constants;
ii) the language of ordered rings Lor = Lr âˆª{<}, where < is a binary
relation symbol;
iii) the language of pure sets L = âˆ…;
iv) the language of graphs is L = {R} where R is a binary relation
symbol.
Next, we describe the structures where L is the appropriate language.
Deï¬nition 1.1.2 An L-structure M is given by the following data:
i) a nonempty set M called the universe, domain, or underlying set of
M;
ii) a function f M : M nf â†’M for each f âˆˆF;
iii) a set RM âŠ†M nR for each R âˆˆR;
iv) an element cM âˆˆM for each c âˆˆC.
We refer to f M, RM, and cM as the interpretations of the symbols f,
R, and c. We often write the structure as M = (M, f M, RM, cM : f âˆˆ
F, R âˆˆR, and c âˆˆC). We will use the notation A, B, M, N, . . . to refer to
the underlying sets of the structures A, B, M, N, . . ..
For example, suppose that we are studying groups. We might use the
language Lg = {Â·, e}, where Â· is a binary function symbol and e is a constant
symbol. An Lg-structure G = (G, Â·G, eG) will be a set G equipped with a
binary relation Â·G and a distinguished element eG. For example, G = (R, Â·, 1)
is an Lg-structure where we interpret Â· as multiplication and e as 1; that
is, Â·G = Â· and eG = 1. Also, N = (N, +, 0) is an Lg-structure where Â·N = +
and eG = 1. Of course, N is not a group, but it is an Lg-structure.
Usually, we will choose languages that closely correspond to the structure
that we wish to study. For example, if we want to study the real numbers
as an ordered ï¬eld, we would use the language of ordered rings Lor and
give each symbol its natural interpretation.
We will study maps that preserve the interpretation of L.
Deï¬nition 1.1.3 Suppose that M and N are L-structures with universes
M and N, respectively. An L-embedding Î· : M â†’N is a one-to-one map
Î· : M â†’N that preserves the interpretation of all of the symbols of L.
More precisely:

R âˆˆR and a1, . . . , amj âˆˆM;
iii) Î·(cM) = cN for c âˆˆC.
A bijective L-embedding is called an L-isomorphism. If M âŠ†N and the
inclusion map is an L-embedding, we say either that M is a substructure
of N or that N is an extension of M.
For example:
i) (Z, +, 0) is a substructure of (R, +, 0).
ii) If Î· : Z â†’R is the function Î·(x) = ex, then Î· is an Lg-embedding of
(Z, +, 0) into (R, Â·, 1).
The cardinality of M is |M|, the cardinality of the universe of M. If
Î· : M â†’N is an embedding then the cardinality of N is at least the
cardinality of M.
We use the language L to create formulas describing properties of L-
structures. Formulas will be strings of symbols built using the symbols of L,
variable symbols v1, v2, . . ., the equality symbol =, the Boolean connectives
âˆ§, âˆ¨, and Â¬, which we read as â€œand,â€ â€œor,â€ and â€œnotâ€, the quantiï¬ers âˆƒ
and âˆ€, which we read as â€œthere existsâ€ and â€œfor allâ€, and parentheses ( , ).
Deï¬nition 1.1.4 The set of L-terms is the smallest set T such that
i) c âˆˆT for each constant symbol c âˆˆC,
ii) each variable symbol vi âˆˆT for i = 1, 2, . . ., and
iii) if t1, . . . , tnf âˆˆT and f âˆˆF, then f(t1, . . . , tnf ) âˆˆT .

Deï¬nition 1.1.5 We say that Ï† is an atomic L-formula if Ï† is either

â€¢ v1 = 0 âˆ¨v1 > 0.
â€¢ âˆƒv2 v2 Â· v2 = v1.

Intuitively, the ï¬rst formula asserts that v1 â‰¥0, the second asserts that
v1 is a square, and the third asserts that every nonzero element has a

to be true in a structure, but these examples already show one diï¬ƒculty.
While in any Lor-structure the third formula will either be true or false,
the ï¬rst two formulas express a property that may or may not be true of
particular elements of the structure. In the Lor-structure (Z, +, âˆ’, Â·, <, 0, 1),
the second formula would be true of 9 but false of 8.
We say that a variable v occurs freely in a formula Ï† if it is not inside a

free in the ï¬rst two formulas and bound in the third, whereas v2 is bound
in both formulas. We call a formula a sentence if it has no free variables.

1.2
Theories
Let L be a language. An L-theory T is simply a set of L-sentences. We say
that M is a model of T and write M |= T if M |= Ï† for all sentences Ï† âˆˆT.
The set T = {âˆ€x x = 0, âˆƒx x Ì¸= 0} is a theory. Because the two sentences
in T are contradictory, there are no models of T. We say that a theory is
satisï¬able if it has a model.
We say that a class of L-structures K is an elementary class if there is
an L-theory T such that K = {M : M |= T}.
One way to get a theory is to take Th(M), the full theory of an L-
structure M. In this case, the elementary class of models of Th(M) is
exactly the class of L-structures elementarily equivalent to M. More typi-
cally, we have a class of structures in mind and try to write a set of prop-
erties T describing these structures. We call these sentences axioms for the
elementary class.
We give a few basic examples of theories and elementary classes that we
will return to frequently.
Example 1.2.1 Inï¬nite Sets
Let L = âˆ….

L-structure M with universe M is a model of T if and only if M is inï¬nite.

â€¢ Consider the natural numbers N as an L = {+, Â·, 0, 1} structure.
The deï¬nable sets are quite complex. For example, there is an L-formula

only if N |= âˆƒs T(e, x, s), so the set of halting computations is deï¬nable. It
is well known that this set is not computable (see, for example, [94]). This
leads to an interesting conclusion.

How do we show that X âŠ‚M n is not deï¬nable? The following proposi-

Proposition 1.3.5 Let M be an L-structure. If X âŠ‚M n is A-deï¬nable,

Corollary 1.3.6 The set of real numbers is not deï¬nable in the ï¬eld of
complex numbers.
Proof If R were deï¬nable, then it would be deï¬nable over a ï¬nite A âŠ‚C.

This proof worked because C has many automorphisms. The situation
is much diï¬€erent for R. Any automorphism of the real ï¬eld must ï¬x the
rational numbers. Because the ordering is deï¬nable it must be preserved by
any automorphism. Because the rationals are dense in R, the only automor-

(there are 22â„µ0 subsets of R and only 2â„µ0 possible deï¬nitions), but we
cannot use Proposition 1.3.5 to show any particular set is undeï¬nable. In

suï¬ƒciently rich models.

Interpretability
It is often very useful to study the structures that can be deï¬ned inside a
given structure. For example, let K be a ï¬eld and G be GL2(K), the group
of invertible 2Ã—2 matrices over K. Let X = {(a, b, c, d) âˆˆK4 : adâˆ’bc Ì¸= 0}.
Let f : X2 â†’X by
f((a1, b1, c1, d1), (a2, b2, c2, d2)) =
(a1a2 + b1c2, a1b2 + b1d2, c1a2 + d1c2, c1b2 + d1d2).
Clearly, X and f are deï¬nable in (K, +, Â·) and the set X with the operation
f is isomorphic to GL2(K), where the identity element of X is (1, 0, 0, 1).
We say that an L0-structure N is deï¬nably interpreted in an L-structure
M if and only if we can ï¬nd a deï¬nable X âŠ†M n for some n and we can
interpret the symbols of L0 as deï¬nable subsets and functions on X (where
â€œdeï¬nableâ€ means deï¬nable using L) so that the resulting L0-structure is
isomorphic to N.
The example above shows that (GLn(K), Â·, e) is deï¬nably interpreted
in (K, +Â·, 0, 1). A linear algebraic group over K is a subgroup of GLn(K)
deï¬ned by polynomial equations over K. It is easy to see that any linear
algebraic group over K is deï¬nably interpreted in K.
For another example, let L = âˆ…and let M be an L-structure where
|M| = â„µ0. Let L0 = {E}, where E is a binary relation. Let N = (N, E) be

size â„µ0. Let

to the cosets of a deï¬nable equivalence relation.

Our treatment of languages, structures, and theories has been very terse.
For a more detailed and leisurely discussion, we refer the reader to [8], [94],
or [31].
If L is a ï¬nite language and T is an L-theory, then we can ï¬nd a theory
T â€² of graphs such that the analog of Lemma 1.3.8 holds. There are also gen-
eralizations for countable languages. See [40] for details. Mekler [70] proved
an analogous result showing that any structure in a countable language can
be interpreted in a nilpotent group.

2
Basic Techniques
2.1
The Compactness Theorem
Let T be an L-theory and Ï† an L-sentence. To show that T |= Ï†, we
must show that Ï† holds in every model of T. Checking all models of T
sounds like a daunting task, but in practice we usually show that T |= Ï†
by giving an informal mathematical proof that Ï† is true in every model of
T. One of the ï¬rst great achievements of mathematical logic was giving a
rigorous deï¬nition of â€œproofâ€ that completely captures the notion of â€œlogical
consequence.â€
A proof of Ï† from T is a ï¬nite sequence of L-formulas Ïˆ1, . . . , Ïˆm such
that Ïˆm = Ï† and Ïˆi âˆˆT or Ïˆi follows from Ïˆ1, . . . , Ïˆiâˆ’1 by a simple logical
rule for each i. We write T âŠ¢Ï† if there is a proof of Ï† from T. Examples
of a â€œsimpleâ€ logical rules are:
â€œfrom Ï† and Ïˆ conclude Ï† âˆ§Ïˆ,â€ or
â€œfrom Ï† âˆ§Ïˆ conclude Ï†.â€
It will not be important for our purposes to go into the details of the
proof system, but we stress the following points. (See [94], for example, for
complete details of one possible proof system.)
â€¢ Proofs are ï¬nite.
â€¢ (Soundness) If T âŠ¢Ï†, then T |= Ï†.
â€¢ If T is a ï¬nite set of sentences, then there is an algorithm that, when
given a sequence of L-formulas Ïƒ and an L-sentence Ï†, will decide whether
Ïƒ is a proof of Ï† from T.

34
2. Basic Techniques
Note that the last point does not say that there is an algorithm that will
decide if T âŠ¢Ï†. It only says that there is an algorithm that can check each
purported proof.
We say that a language L is recursive if there is an algorithm that decides
whether a sequence of symbols is an L-formula. We say that an L-theory
T is recursive if there is an algorithm that, when given an L-sentence Ï† as
input, decides whether Ï† âˆˆT.
Proposition 2.1.1 If L is a recursive language and T is a recursive L-
theory, then {Ï† : T âŠ¢Ï†} is recursively enumerable; that is, there is an
algorithm, that when given Ï† as input will halt accepting if T âŠ¢Ï† and not
halt if T Ì¸âŠ¢Ï†.
Proof There is Ïƒ0, Ïƒ1, Ïƒ2, . . ., a computable listing of all ï¬nite sequences
of L-formulas. At stage i of our algorithm, we check to see whether Ïƒi is
a proof of Ïˆ from T. This involves checking that each formula either is in
T (which we can check because T is recursive) or follows by a logical rule
from earlier formulas in the sequence Ïƒi and that the last formula is Ï†. If
Ïƒi is a proof of Ï† from T, then we halt accepting; otherwise we go on to
stage i + 1.
Remarkably, the ï¬nitistic syntactic notion of â€œproofâ€ completely captures
the semantic notion of â€œlogical consequence.â€
Theorem 2.1.2 (GÂ¨odelâ€™s Completeness Theorem) Let T be an L-
theory and Ï† an L-sentence, then T |= Ï† if and only if T âŠ¢Ï†.
The Completeness Theorem gives a criterion for testing whether an
L-theory is satisï¬able. We say that an L-theory T is inconsistent if
T âŠ¢(Ï† âˆ§Â¬Ï†) for some sentence Ï†; otherwise we say that T is consistent.
Because our proof system is sound, any satisï¬able theory is consistent. The
Completeness Theorem implies that the converse is true.
Corollary 2.1.3 T is consistent if and only if T is satisï¬able.
Proof Suppose that T is not satisï¬able. Because there are no models of
T, every model of T is a model of (Ï† âˆ§Â¬Ï†). Thus, T |= (Ï† âˆ§Â¬Ï†) and by
the Completeness Theorem T âŠ¢(Ï† âˆ§Â¬Ï†).
This has a deceptively simple consequence.
Theorem 2.1.4 (Compactness Theorem) T is satisï¬able if and only
if every ï¬nite subset of T is satisï¬able.
Proof
Clearly, if T is satisï¬able, then every subset of T is satisï¬able.
On the other hand, if T is not satisï¬able, then T is inconsistent. Let Ïƒ be
a proof of a contradiction from T. Because Ïƒ is ï¬nite, only ï¬nitely many
assumptions from T are used in the proof. Thus, there is a ï¬nite T0 âŠ†T

2.1 The Compactness Theorem
35
such that Ïƒ is a proof of a contradiction from T0. But then T0 is a ï¬nite
unsatisï¬able subset of T.
Although it is a simple consequence of the Completeness Theorem and
the ï¬nite nature of proof, the Compactness Theorem is the cornerstone of
model theory. Because it will not be useful for us to understand the exact
nature of our proof system, we will not prove the Completeness Theorem.
Instead we will give a second proof of the Compactness Theorem that
does not appeal directly to the Completeness Theorem. This proof can be
adapted to prove the Completeness Theorem as well.
Henkin Constructions
We say that a theory T is ï¬nitely satisï¬able if every ï¬nite subset of T is
satisï¬able. We will show that every ï¬nitely satisï¬able theory T is satisï¬able.
To do this, we must build a model of T. The main idea of the construction
is that we will add enough constants to the language so that every element
of our model will be named by a constant symbol. The following deï¬nition
will give us suï¬ƒcient conditions to construct a model from the constants.
Deï¬nition 2.1.5
We say that an L-theory T has the witness property
if whenever Ï†(v) is an L-formula with one free variable v, then there is a
constant symbol c âˆˆL such that T |= (âˆƒv Ï†(v)) â†’Ï†(c).
An L-theory T is maximal if for all Ï† either Ï† âˆˆT or Â¬Ï† âˆˆT.
Our proof will frequently use the following simple lemma.

âˆ†âŠ†T is ï¬nite and âˆ†|= Ïˆ, then Ïˆ âˆˆT.
Proof If Ïˆ Ì¸âˆˆT, then, because T is maximal, Â¬Ïˆ âˆˆT. But then âˆ†âˆª{Â¬Ïˆ}
is a ï¬nite unsatisï¬able subset of T, a contradiction.

2.2
Complete Theories
Deï¬nition 2.2.1 An L-theory T is called complete if for any L-sentence
Ï† either T |= Ï† or T |= Â¬Ï†.
For M an L-structure, then the full theory
Th(M) = {Ï† : Ï† is an L-sentence and M |= Ï†}
is a complete theory. Usually, it is diï¬ƒcult to determine exactly what sen-
tences are in Th(M). In many cases, the key to understanding Th(M) is

case, M |= Ï† if and only if T |= Ï†, because if T Ì¸|= Ï†, then T |= Â¬Ï† and
M |= Â¬Ï†.
The Compactness Theorem yields a very useful completeness test that we
will use in this section to show that several natural theories are complete.
This will have striking consequences for algebraically closed ï¬elds.

Proposition 2.2.4 The theory of torsion-free divisible Abelian groups is
Îº-categorical for all Îº > â„µ0.
Proof We ï¬rst argue that models of T are essentially vector spaces over
the ï¬eld of rational numbers Q. Clearly, if V is any vector space over Q,
then the underlying additive group of V is a model of T. On the other
hand, if G |= T, g âˆˆG, and n âˆˆN with n > 0, we can ï¬nd h âˆˆG such that
nh = g. If nk = g, then n(h âˆ’k) = 0. Because G is torsion-free there is a

as a Q-vector space under the action m
n g = m(g/n).
Two Q-vector spaces are isomorphic if and only if they have the same
dimension. Thus, models of T are determined up to isomorphism by their
dimension. If G has dimension Î», then |G| = Î» + â„µ0. If Îº is uncountable
and G has cardinality Îº, then G has dimension Îº. Thus, for Îº > â„µ0 any
two models of T of cardinality Îº are isomorphic.
Note that T is not â„µ0-categorical. Indeed, there are â„µ0 nonisomorphic
models corresponding to vector spaces of dimension 1, 2, 3, . . . and â„µ0.
A similar argument applies to the theory of algebraically closed ï¬elds.
Let ACFp be the theory of algebraically closed ï¬elds of characteristic p,
where p is either 0 or a prime number.
Proposition 2.2.5 ACFp is Îº-categorical for all uncountable cardinals Îº.
Proof
Two algebraically closed ï¬elds are isomorphic if and only if they
have the same characteristic and transcendence degree (see, for example
[58] X Â§1). An algebraically closed ï¬eld of transcendence degree Î» has car-
dinality Î» + â„µ0. If Îº > â„µ0, an algebraically closed ï¬eld of cardinality Îº also
has transcendence degree Îº. Thus, any two algebraically closed ï¬elds of the
same characteristic and same uncountable cardinality are isomorphic.
We give two simpler examples.
â€¢ Let L be the empty language. Then the theory of an inï¬nite set is
Îº-categorical for all cardinals Îº.
â€¢ Let L = {E}, where E is a binary relation, and let T be the theory of
an equivalence relation with exactly two classes, both of which are inï¬nite.
It is easy to see that any two countable models of T are isomorphic. On
the other hand, T is not Îº-categorical for Îº > â„µ0. To see this, let M0 be a
model where both classes have cardinality Îº, and let M1 be a model where
one class has cardinality Îº and the other has cardinality â„µ0. Clearly, M0
and M1 are not isomorphic.

Theorem 2.2.6 (Vaughtâ€™s Test) Let T be a satisï¬able theory with no
ï¬nite models that is Îº-categorical for some inï¬nite cardinal Îº â‰¥|L|. Then
T is complete.
Proof Suppose that T is not complete. Then there is a sentence Ï† such that
T Ì¸|= Ï† and T Ì¸|= Â¬Ï†. Because T Ì¸|= Ïˆ if and only if T âˆª{Â¬Ïˆ} is satisï¬able,
the theories T0 = T âˆª{Ï†} and T1 = T âˆª{Â¬Ï†} are satisï¬able. Because T has
no ï¬nite models, both T0 and T1 have inï¬nite models. By Proposition 2.2.2
we can ï¬nd M0 and M1 of cardinality Îº with Mi |= Ti. Because M0 and
M1 disagree about Ï†, there are not elementarily equivalent and hence, by
Theorem 1.1.10, nonisomorphic. This contradicts the Îº-categoricity of T.
The assumption that T has no ï¬nite models is necessary. Suppose that
T is the {+, 0}-theory of groups, where every element has order 2. In the
exercises, we will show that T is Îº-categorical for all Îº â‰¥â„µ0. However, T
is not complete. The sentence âˆƒxâˆƒyâˆƒz (x Ì¸= y âˆ§y Ì¸= z âˆ§z Ì¸= x) is false in
the two-element group but true in every other model of T.
Vaughtâ€™s test implies that all of the categorical theories discussed above
are complete. In particular, algebraically closed ï¬elds are complete. This
result of Tarski has several immediate interesting consequences.
Deï¬nition 2.2.7
We say that an L-theory T is decidable if there is an
algorithm that when given an L-sentence Ï† as input decides whether T |= Ï†.
Lemma 2.2.8 Let T be a recursive complete satisï¬able theory in a recur-
sive language L. Then T is decidable.
Proof Because T is satisï¬able A = {Ï† : T |= Ï†} and B = {Ï† : T |= Â¬Ï†}
are disjoint. Because T is consistent A âˆªB is the set of all L-sentences. By
the Completeness Theorem, A = {Ï† : T âŠ¢Ï†} and B = {Ï† : T âŠ¢Â¬Ï†}. By
Proposition 2.1.1 A and B are recursively enumerable. But any recursively
enumerable set with a recursively enumerable complement is recursive.
Informally, to decide whether Ï† is a logical consequence of a complete
satisï¬able recursive theory T, we begin searching through possible proofs
from T until we ï¬nd either a proof of Ï† or a proof of Â¬Ï†. Because T is
satisï¬able, we will not ï¬nd proofs of both. Because T is complete, we will
eventually ï¬nd a proof of one or the other.
Corollary 2.2.9 For p = 0 or p prime, ACFp is decidable. In particular,
Th(C), the ï¬rst-order theory of the ï¬eld of complex numbers, is decidable.
The completeness of ACFp can also be thought of as a ï¬rst-order version
of the Lefschetz Principle from algebraic geometry.

lowing are equivalent.
i) Ï† is true in the complex numbers.

2. Basic Techniques
2.3
Up and Down
In Chapter 1, we looked at the category of L-structures and introduced
the notion of L-embeddings between structures. These are the maps that
preserve the relations, constants, and functions of L. Often we will want
to consider the more restrictive class of maps that preserve all ï¬rst-order
properties.
Deï¬nition 2.3.1
If M and N are L-structures, then an L-embedding
j : M â†’N is called an elementary embedding if
M |= Ï†(a1, . . . , an) â‡”N |= Ï†(j(a1), . . . , j(an))
for all L-formulas Ï†(v1, . . . , vn) and all a1, . . . , an âˆˆM.
If M is a substructure of N, we say that it is an elementary substructure
and write M â‰ºN if the inclusion map is elementary. We also say that N
is an elementary extension of M.
The proof of Theorem 1.1.10 shows that isomorphisms are elementary
maps.
Next we give a way to construct embeddings and elementary embeddings.
Deï¬nition 2.3.2
Suppose that M is an L-structure. Let LM be the
language where we add to L constant symbols m for each element of M.
The atomic diagram of M is {Ï†(m1, . . . , mn) : Ï† is either an atomic L-
formula or the negation of an atomic L-formula and M |= Ï†(m1, . . . , mn)}.
The elementary diagram of M is
{Ï†(m1, . . . , mn) : M |= Ï†(m1, . . . , mn), Ï† an L-formula}.
We let Diag(M) and Diagel(M) denote the atomic and elementary dia-
grams of M, respectively.
Lemma 2.3.3 i) Suppose that N is an LM-structure and N |= Diag(M);
then, viewing N as an L-structure, there is an L-embedding of M into N.
ii) If N |= Diagel(M), then there is an elementary embedding of M into
N.
Proof i) Let j : M â†’N by j(m) = mN ; that is, j(m) is the interpretation
of this constant symbol m in N. If m1, m2 are distinct elements of M, then
m1 Ì¸= m2 âˆˆDiag(M); thus, j(m1) Ì¸= j(m2) so j is an embedding. If f is a
function symbol of L and f M(m1, . . . , mn) = mn+1, then f(m1, . . . , mn) =
mn+1 is a formula in Diag(M) and f N (j(m1), . . . , j(mn)) = j(mn+1). If
R is a relation symbol and m âˆˆRM, then R(m1, . . . , mn) âˆˆDiag(M) and
(j(m1), . . . , j(mn)) âˆˆRN . Hence, j is an L-embedding.
ii) If N |= Diagel(M), then the map j above is elementary.
Combining these observations with the Compactness Theorem allows us
to build elementary extensions.

The theory T is very interesting because it gives us insights into random
ï¬nite graphs. Let GN be the set of all graphs with vertices {1, 2, . . . , N}.
We consider a probability measure on GN where we make all graphs equally
likely. This is the same as constructing a random graph where we indepen-

The type of back-and-forth constructions we did in Theorems 2.4.1 and
2.4.2 will appear several times in the book. It is useful to recast construc-
tions as games. We will do this in a bit more generality. Let L be a language
and M = (M, . . .) and N = (N, . . .) be two L-structures with M âˆ©N = âˆ….
If A âŠ†M, B âŠ†N and f : A â†’B, we say that f is a partial embedding
if f âˆª{(cM, cN ) : c a constant of L} is a bijection preserving all relations
and functions of L.
We will deï¬ne an inï¬nite two-player game GÏ‰(M, N). We will call the
two players player I and player II; together they will build a partial embed-
ding f from M to N. A play of the game will consist of Ï‰ stages. At the
ith-stage, player I moves ï¬rst and either plays mi âˆˆM, challenging player
II to put mi into the domain of f, or ni âˆˆN, challenging player II to put ni
into the range. If player I plays mi âˆˆM, then player II must play ni âˆˆN,
whereas if player I plays ni âˆˆM, then player II must play mi âˆˆM. Player
II wins the play of the game if f = {(mi, ni) : i = 1, 2, . . .} is the graph of
a partial embedding.
A strategy for player II in GÏ‰(M, N) is a function Ï„ such that if player Iâ€™s
ï¬rst n moves are c1, . . . , cn, then player IIâ€™s nth move will be Ï„(c1, . . . , cn).
We say that player II uses the strategy Ï„ in the play of the game if the play
looks like:
Player I
Player II
c1
Ï„(c1)
c2
Ï„(c1, c2)
c3
Ï„(c1, c2, c3)
...
...
We say that Ï„ is a winning strategy for player II, if for any sequence of
plays c1, c2, . . . player I makes, player II will win by following Ï„. We deï¬ne
strategies for player I analogously.
For example, suppose that M, N |= DLO. Then, player II has a winning
strategy. Suppose that up to stage n they have built a partial embedding
g : A â†’B. If player I plays a âˆˆM, then player II plays b âˆˆN such that
the cut b makes in B is the image of the cut of a in A under g. Similarly, if

Proposition 2.4.5 If M and N are countable, then the second player has
a winning strategy in GÏ‰(M, N) if and only if M âˆ¼= N.
Proof
If M and N are isomorphic, then player II can win by playing
according to the isomorphism.
Suppose that player II has a winning strategy. Let m0, m1, . . . list M and
n0, n1, . . . list N. Consider a play of the game where the second player uses
the winning strategy and the ï¬rst player plays m0, n0, m1, n1, m2, n2, . . ..
If f is the partial embedding built during this play of the game then the
domain of f is M and the range of f is N. Thus, f is an isomorphism.
By weakening the game, we can, for suitable languages, give a character-
ization of elementary equivalence. Fix L a ï¬nite language with no function
symbols, and let M and N be L-structures. We deï¬ne a game Gn(M, N)
for n = 1, 2, . . .. The game will have n rounds. On the ith round player I
plays ï¬rst and either plays ai âˆˆM or bi âˆˆN. On player IIâ€™s turn, if player
I played ai âˆˆM, then player II must play bi âˆˆN, and if player I plays
bi âˆˆN, then player II must play ai âˆˆM. The game stops after the nth
round. Player II wins if {(ai, bi) : i = 1, . . . , n} is the graph of a partial
embedding from M into N. We call Gn(M, N) an Ehrenfeuchtâ€“FraÂ¨Ä±ssÂ´e
game.
Our goal is to prove the following theorem.
Theorem 2.4.6 Let L be a ï¬nite language without function symbols and
let M and N be L-structures. Then, M â‰¡N if and only if the second
player has a winning strategy in Gn(M, N) for all n.
Before proving this, we will need several lemmas.
Lemma 2.4.7 One of the players has a winning strategy in Gn(M, N).
Proof (sketch) This follows from Zermeloâ€™s theorem that in any two-person
ï¬nite length game of perfect information without ties one of the players has
a winning strategy (see [10] 1.7.1). It also follows from the determinacy of
closed games (see [52]). We outline the proof. Suppose that player II does
not have a winning strategy. Then, there is some move player I can make
in round one so that player II has no move available to force a win. Player
I makes that move. Now, whatever player II does, there is still a move that
if made by player I means that player II cannot force a win. Player I makes
that move and continues in this way. On the last round, there is still a
move possible so that player II has no winning move. Player I makes that
move and wins. This informally describes a winning strategy for player I
(the strategy can be summarized as â€œavoid losing positionsâ€).

Remarks
The Compactness Theorem was proved for countable languages by GÂ¨odel.
Malcev proved the general result and was the ï¬rst who saw its power. In-
deed in a review of a paper of Skolemâ€™s, GÂ¨odel [35] points out that the
existence of a nonstandard model of arithmetic follows from the Incom-
pleteness Theorem, but does not mention the simple compactness proof.
The completeness and decidability of ACFp for p = 0 or prime was ï¬rst
proved by Tarski using the method of quantiï¬er elimination from Chapter
3.
Axâ€™s Theorem 2.2.11 was ï¬rst proved as we described above. Later, Borel
[13] gave a topological proof and Rudin [90] gave an algebraic proof.
The method of diagrams from Section 3.2 is due to Abraham Robinson.
Theorem 2.3.9 is due to 	LoÂ´s and Tarski. Theorem 2.3.11 is due to Tarski
and Vaught.
The analysis of the random graph is due to Fagin. See [97] for more
on zero-one laws for random graphs where we consider other probability
measures. These ideas can easily be generalized to study a random n-ary
relation. Constructions of this sort are generally referred to as FraÂ¨Ä±ssÂ´e con-
structions. This topic is discussed more carefully in [40]. Hrushovski [41],

2.5 Exercises and Remarks
69
[42] has expanded these ideas into a powerful tool for constructing inter-
esting new structures.
Ehrenfeuchtâ€“FraÂ¨Ä±ssÂ´e games, and their variants, play an interesting role
in ï¬nite model theory (see, for example, [32]). The use of games and back-
and-forth systems to understand inï¬nitary equivalence is due to Karp. The
use of LÏ‰1,Ï‰ to characterize countable structures and Exercise 2.5.35 are
due to Scott. The reader is referred to [72] for a survey of the model theory
of inï¬nitary logic and its connection to classical model theory.
Exercise 2.5.15 is due to Chang, 	LoÂ´s, and Suszko. The ï¬nitely axiomatiz-
able â„µ1-categorical theory given in Exercise 2.5.16 is Morleyâ€™s simpliï¬cation
of an example by Peretjatâ€™kin. Exercise 2.5.17 is due to LindstrÂ¨om.
Ultraproducts and ultrapowers are an important tool in model theory,
although they will not play a central role in this text. A very thorough
development of the model theory of ultraproducts is contained in [22]. A
high point of the theory is the Keislerâ€“Shelah Theorem (see [22] 6.1.15).
Theorem 2.5.36 (Keislerâ€“Shelah Theorem) Two
L-structures
M
and N are elementarily equivalent if and only if there is an index set I
and an ultraï¬lter D on I such that & M/D âˆ¼=
& N/D.
The study of ultraproducts of ï¬nite ï¬elds mentioned in Exercise 2.5.21 is
a fascinating one initiated by Ax [2] and developed further by Chatzidakis,
van den Dries, and Macintyre [23].
The examples from Exercise 2.5.28 are due to Ehrenfeucht. The analysis
of elementary equivalence of ordinals is due to Mostowski and Tarski.

3.1
Quantiï¬er Elimination
The study of deï¬nable sets is often made quite complicated by quantiï¬ers.
For example, in the structure (N, +, Â·, <) the quantiï¬er-free deï¬nable sets
are deï¬ned by polynomial equations and inequalities. Even if we use only
existential quantiï¬ers the deï¬nable sets become complicated. By the Mati-
jaseviË‡câ€“Robinsonâ€“Davisâ€“Putnam solution to Hilbertâ€™s 10th problem [24],
every recursively enumerable subset of N is deï¬ned by a formula
âˆƒv1 . . . âˆƒvn p(x, v1, . . . , vn) = 0
for some polynomial p âˆˆN[X, Y1, . . . , Yn]. As we allow more alternations
of quantiï¬ers, we get even more complicated deï¬nable sets.
Not surprisingly, it will be easiest to study deï¬nable sets that are deï¬ned
by quantiï¬er-free formulas. Sometimes formulas with quantiï¬ers can be
shown to be equivalent to formulas without quantiï¬ers. Here are two well-
known examples. Let Ï†(a, b, c) be the formula
âˆƒx ax2 + bx + c = 0.

Theorem 3.1.9 DAG has quantiï¬er elimination.
Proof Suppose that G0 and G1 are torsion-free divisible Abelian groups,

Quantiï¬er elimination gives us a good picture of the deï¬nable sets in
a model of DAG. Suppose that Ï†(v1, . . . , vn, w1, . . . , wm) is an atomic for-

L-formulas, every deï¬nable subset of Gn is a Boolean combination of hy-

In particular, suppose that a âˆˆGm and Ï†(v, a) deï¬nes a subset of G.
The â€œhyperplanesâ€ in G are just single points. Thus, {g âˆˆG : G |= Ï†(g, a)}
is either ï¬nite or coï¬nite. Thus, every deï¬nable subset of G was already
deï¬nable already in the language of equality.1 This is an example of a very
important phenomenon.

Deï¬nition 3.1.10 We say that an L-theory T is strongly minimal if for

If T is a theory then Tâˆ€is the set of all universal consequences of T. In

We say that a theory T has algebraically prime models if for any A |= Tâˆ€
there is M |= T and an embedding i : A â†’M such that for all N |= T and
embeddings j : A â†’N there is h : M â†’N such that j = h â—¦i. Lemma

N |= âˆƒw Ï†(a, w) then so does M. Lemma 3.1.7 says that if G and H are
models of DAG and G âŠ†H, then G â‰ºs H.
The proof of Theorem 3.1.9 can be easily modiï¬ed to yield the following

Corollary 3.1.12 Suppose that T is an L-theory such that

M âŠ†N and M, N |= T.

Presburger Arithmetic
We conclude this chapter by considering a slightly more complicated ex-
ample. Let L = {+, âˆ’, <, 0, 1} and consider the L-theory of the ordered
group of integers. In fact this theory will not have quantiï¬er elimination in
the language L. Let Ïˆn(v) be the formula
âˆƒy v = y + . . . + y

obstruction to quantiï¬er elimination. Let Lâˆ—= L âˆª{Pn : n = 2, 3, . . .},
where Pn is a unary predicate which we will interpret as the elements

and is decidable. Because we are only adding predicates for sets that we
could deï¬ne already in the language L, we will not change the deï¬nable
sets (see Exercise 1.4.15).
There is something slippery going on here that we should be careful
about. For any language L and L-theory T, there is a language Lâ€² âŠ‡L
and an Lâ€²-theory T â€² âŠ‡T such that for any M |= T we can interpret the

In general, this is a completely useless construction. Quantiï¬er elimina-

deï¬ned using L, we would have the same problems with sets deï¬ned using
Lâ€².

We have provided a number of proofs of quantiï¬er elimination without

that T |= âˆ€v (Ï†(v) â†”Ïˆ(v)). Because T is decidable this is an eï¬€ective
search. Because T has quantiï¬er elimination, we will eventually ï¬nd Ïˆ.

Quantiï¬er elimination has a geometric interpretation. To state this, we

be a ï¬eld. If S âŠ†K[X1, . . . , Xn], let V (S) = {a âˆˆKn : p(a) = 0 for all
p âˆˆS}. If Y âŠ†Kn, we let I(Y ) = {f âˆˆK[X1, . . . , Xn] : f(a) = 0 for
all a âˆˆY }. We say that X âŠ†Kn is Zariski closed if X = V (S) for some
S âŠ†K[X1, . . . , Xn]. We summarize some basic facts about Zariski closed
sets. For more details, see [37] or [91].

Elimination of Imaginaries in Algebraically Closed Fields
We conclude by studying deï¬nable equivalence relations in algebraically
closed ï¬elds. In Section 1.3, we remarked that quotient structures are
frequently important in mathematics. For example, if G is a group and
H is a subgroup, we study the coset space G/H. If G is a deï¬nable
group and H is a deï¬nable subgroup, then the equivalence relation xEy if
and only if y âˆˆHx is a deï¬nable equivalence relation. Another example
that arises in algebraic geometry is the construction of projective space.
If K is a ï¬eld, deï¬ne the equivalence relation âˆ¼on Kn+1 \ {0}, where
(x0, . . . , xn) âˆ¼(y0, . . . , yn) if and only if there is a nonzero Î» âˆˆK such
that yi = Î»xi for i = 0, . . . , n. The equivalence relation âˆ¼is deï¬nable and
the quotient is Pn
K = Kn+1 \ {0}mod âˆ¼, projective n-space over K.
Our goal is to show that quotients are constructible. In particular, we
will show that if E is a deï¬nable equivalence relation on Kn, where K is
algebraically closed, then there is a deï¬nable function f : Kn â†’Km for
some m such that xEy if and only if f(x) = f(y). Thus, we can identify
the quotient Kn/E with the image of f. In other words it allows us to view
the quotient of a constructible set by a constructible equivalence relation
as a constructible set.
We begin with a very important special case. Let k be a ï¬eld (in this
special case we will not need to assume that k is algebraically closed). We
can think of x âˆˆknm as a sequence (x1, . . . , xm), where xi âˆˆkn. Let E be
the equivalence relation xEy if and only if (x1, . . . , xn) is a permutation of
(y1, . . . , yn). Clearly, E is a deï¬nable equivalence relation.

3.3
Real Closed Fields
In this section, we will concentrate on the ï¬eld of real numbers. Unlike
algebraically closed ï¬elds, the theory of the real numbers does not have
quantiï¬er elimination in Lr, the language of rings. The proof of Corollary
3.2.9 shows that any ï¬eld with quantiï¬er elimination is strongly minimal,
whereas in R, if Ï†(x) is the formula âˆƒz z2 = x, then Ï† deï¬nes an inï¬-
nite coinï¬nite deï¬nable set (see also Exercise 3.4.24). In 7.2 we will see
that algebraically closed ï¬elds are the only inï¬nite ï¬elds with quantiï¬er
elimination in the language of rings.
In fact, the ordering is the only obstruction to quantiï¬er elimination. We
will eventually analyze the real numbers in the language Lor and show that
we have quantiï¬er elimination in this language. Because the ordering x < y
is deï¬nable in the real ï¬eld by the formula
âˆƒz (z Ì¸= 0 âˆ§x + z2 = y),
any subset of Rn deï¬nable using an Lor-formula is already deï¬nable using
an Lr-formula (see Exercise 1.4.15). We will see that quantiï¬er elimination
in Lor leads us to a good geometric understanding of the deï¬nable sets.
We begin by reviewing some of the necessary algebraic background on
ordered ï¬elds. All of the algebraic results stated in this chapter are due to
Artin and Schreier and proved in Appendix B.
Deï¬nition 3.3.1
We say that a ï¬eld F is orderable if there is a linear
order < of F making (F, <) an ordered ï¬eld.
Although there are unique orderings of the ï¬elds R and Q, orderable ï¬elds
may have many possible orderings. The ï¬eld of rational functions Q(X) has
2â„µ0 distinct orderings. To see this, let x be any real number transcendental

3. Algebraic Examples

of the reals to an ordering Q(X) by f(X) < g(X) if and only if f(x) < g(x).
Because X < q if and only if x < q, choosing a diï¬€erent transcendental real
would yield a diï¬€erent ordering. These are not the only orderings. We will

or inï¬nitesimally close to a rational.
There is a purely algebraic characterization of the orderable ï¬elds.
Deï¬nition 3.3.2 We say that F is formally real
if âˆ’1 is not a sum of
squares.
In any ordered ï¬eld all squares are nonnegative. Thus, every orderable

true.
Theorem 3.3.3 If F is a formally real ï¬eld, then F is orderable. Indeed,
if a âˆˆF and âˆ’a is not a sum of squares of elements of F, then there is an
ordering of F where a is positive.
Because the ï¬eld of complex numbers is the only proper algebraic ex-
tension of the real ï¬eld, the real numbers have no proper formally real
algebraic extensions. Fields with this property will play a key role.
Deï¬nition 3.3.4
A ï¬eld F is real closed if it is formally real with no
proper formally real algebraic extensions.
Although it is not obvious at ï¬rst that real closed ï¬elds form an ele-

Semialgebraic Sets
Quantiï¬er elimination for real closed ï¬elds has a geometric interpretation.
Deï¬nition 3.3.17

Corollary 3.3.18 (Tarskiâ€“Seidenberg Theorem) The
semialgebraic

Theorem 3.3.31 (Cell Decomposition) Let X âŠ†F m be semialgebraic.
There are ï¬nitely many pairwise disjoint cells C1, . . . , Cn such that X =
C1 âˆª. . . âˆªCn.
Proof (for m = 2) For each a âˆˆF, let
Ca = {x : âˆ€Ïµ > 0âˆƒy, z âˆˆ(x âˆ’Ïµ, x + Ïµ) [(a, y) âˆˆX âˆ§(a, z) Ì¸âˆˆX]}.
We call Ca the critical values above a. By o-minimality, there are only
ï¬nitely many critical values above a. By uniform bounding, there is a nat-
ural number N such that for all a âˆˆF, |Ca| â‰¤N. We partition F into
A0, A1, . . . , AN, where An = {a : |Ca| = n}.
For each n â‰¤N, we have a deï¬nable function fn : A1 âˆª. . . âˆªAn â†’F by
fn(a) = nth element of Ca. As above, Xa = {y : (a, y) âˆˆX}.
For n â‰¤N and a âˆˆAn, we deï¬ne Pa âˆˆ22n+1, the pattern of X above a,
as follows.
If n = 0, then Pa(0) = 1 if and only if Xa = F. Suppose that n > 0.
Pa(0) = 1 if and only if x âˆˆXa for all x < f1(a).
Pa(2i âˆ’1) = 1 if and only if fi(a) âˆˆX.
For i < n, Pa(2i) = 1 if and only if x âˆˆXa for all x âˆˆ(fi(a), fi+1(a)).
P(2n) = 1 if and only if x âˆˆXa for all x > fn(a).

For each possible pattern Ïƒ âˆˆ22n+1, let An,Ïƒ = {a âˆˆAn : Pa = Ïƒ}.

Remarks
Tarski ï¬rst showed completeness and decidability for the ï¬elds of real and
complex numbers. His proof gave an explicit algorithm for eliminating
quantiï¬ers. Robinson showed that quantiï¬er-elimination results could be
proved by ï¬nding the right embedding theorems. These ideas were fur-
ther extended by Blum. Robinson also introduced the notion of model-
completeness and saw how it could be used to prove Hilbertâ€™s Nullstel-
lensatz and answer Hilbertâ€™s 17th-problem. All of the results on model-
complete theories developed in Exercises 3.4.12 and 3.4.13 are also due to
Robinson.
The completeness and decidability of Presburger arithmetic is due to
Presburger, although the proof of quantiï¬er elimination given here is due
to van den Dries. Theorem 3.2.20 is due to Poizat [84], although the proof
given here is due to Lascar and Pillay.
The positive quantiï¬er-elimination result of Exercise 3.4.21 is due to van
den Dries [28], who showed how it could be used to prove the completeness
of projective varieties and show that any closed semialgebraic set can be
built up from unions and intersections of semialgebraic sets of the form
{x : f(x) â‰¥0}.
The real Nullstellensatz was originally proved by Krivine. His model-
theoretic proof was not noticed by real algebraic geometers, and the result
was proved again later by Dubois and Risler.
Model-completeness and
quantiï¬er elimination have many applications in real algebraic geometry
(see for example [12]). Exercises 3.4.33 and 3.4.34 on o-minimal ordered
groups and ï¬elds are due to Pillay and Steinhorn. [83] Exercise 3.4.36 is
due to Shepherdson. There are many algebraic constructions of models of
open induction (see, for example [61]).
O-minimality was introduced by van den Dries, Pillay, and Steinhorn.
Surprisingly, o-minimal structures have many of the good topological and
geometric properties of strongly minimal sets. This material is developed
carefully in [29].
Once we know that the theories of the real and complex ï¬elds are de-
cidable, it is natural to wonder about the computational complexity of
these theories. Our proofs of decidability give no complexity information,

112
3. Algebraic Examples
but more direct proofs do yield concrete bounds. Collins gave an explicit
quantiï¬er-elimination procedure using cylindric decomposition. His proof
leads to a doubly exponential upper bound on the complexity. Although
there are some good algorithms for attacking speciï¬c subproblems, Fischer
and Rabin showed that the decision problem is exponentially hard. Indeed,
they proved that there are exponential lower bounds even for the theory of
nontrivial torsion-free divisible Abelian groups. See [21] for a collection of
fundamental papers on these issues.
Tarski asked whether the theory of the structure Rexp = (R, +, Â·, exp, <
, 0, 1) is decidable. In the early 1980s, van den Dries recast this question,
asking whether this structure was o-minimal. This was answered positively
by Wilkie [103].
Theorem 3.4.37 The theory of Rexp is model-complete and o-minimal.
The decidability of the theory of Rexp is still an open question, but Mac-
intyre and Wilkie [62] have given a partial positive result.
Conjecture 3.4.38 (Schanuelâ€™s Conjecture) If Î»1, . . . Î»n âˆˆC are lin-
early independent over R, then the ï¬eld Q(Î»1, . . . , Î»n, eÎ»1, . . . , eÎ»n) has
transcendence degree at least n.
Theorem 3.4.39 If Schanuelâ€™s Conjecture is true, then Th(Rexp) is de-
cidable.
Although we know that Rexp is model-complete, it does not have quanti-
ï¬er elimination. Quantiï¬er elimination was proved by van den Dries, Macin-
tyre, and Marker [30] if we add log and all restrictions of analytic functions
to compact sets.
Because Th(Rexp) is model complete it must have a âˆ€âˆƒ-axiomatization.
At present we have no clue what such an axiomatization might look like,
even assuming Schanuelâ€™s Conjecture.
Ax, Kochen, and Ersov
[3], [4], [5], and [55] investigated the model
theory of the p-adic ï¬eld. If K is a valued ï¬eld, let K be the residue ï¬eld,
and G(K) be the value group.
Theorem 3.4.40 i) Let K and L be Henselian valued ï¬elds of character-
istic zero. Then, K â‰¡L if and only if K â‰¡L and G(K) â‰¡G(L).
ii) The theory of Qp is decidable. It is exactly the theory of Henselian
ï¬elds of characteristic zero with residue ï¬eld Fp and value group a model
of Presburger arithmetic.
One important corollary is that if D is a nonprincipal ultraï¬lter on the
set of prime numbers, then & Qp/D âˆ¼= & Fp((t)), where Fp((t)) is the
ï¬eld of formal Laurent series over Fp. This corollary was used to settle the
following conjecture of Artin.

3.4 Exercises and Remarks
113
Corollary 3.4.41 For any integer d, there is M > 0 such that if p > M is
prime and f âˆˆQp[X1, . . . , Xn] is a homogeneous polynomial of degree d <
âˆšn, then there are x1, . . . , xn âˆˆQp not all zero such that f(x1, . . . , xn) = 0.
Macintyre [60] showed that Qp has quantiï¬er elimination if we add a
predicate Pn for the nth-powers for n = 2, 3, . . .. Macintyreâ€™s quantiï¬er
elimination was shown to have interesting applications by Denef [25] who
used it to prove the rationality of certain p-adic zeta functions.
The model theory of modules is another interesting area that we will not
discuss. The interested reader should consult [87].

4.1
Types
Suppose that M is an L-structure and A âŠ†M. Let LA be the language
obtained by adding to L constant symbols for each a âˆˆA. We can naturally
view M as an LA-structure by interpreting the new symbols in the obvious
way. Let ThA(M) be the set of all LA-sentences true in M. Note that
ThA(M) âŠ†Diagel(M).
Deï¬nition 4.1.1
Let p be the set of LA-formulas in free variables
v1, . . . , vn. We call p an n-type if p âˆªThA(M) is satisï¬able. We say that
p is a complete n-type if Ï† âˆˆp or Â¬Ï† âˆˆp for all LA-formulas Ï† with free
variables from v1, . . . , vn. We let SM
n (A) be the set of all complete n-types.
We sometimes refer to incomplete types as partial types. Also, we often
write p(v1, . . . , vn) to stress that p is an n-type.
By the Compactness Theorem, we could replace â€œsatisï¬ableâ€ by â€œï¬nitely
satisï¬ableâ€ in Deï¬nition 4.1.1.
Consider the example M = (Q, <) where A is the set of natural numbers.
Let p(v) be the set of formulas {v > 1, v > 2, v > 3, . . .}. If âˆ†is a ï¬nite
subset of p(v) âˆªThA(M), then we see that âˆ†is satisï¬able by interpreting
v as a suï¬ƒciently large element of Q. By the Compactness Theorem, p(v)âˆª
ThA(M) is satisï¬able and p(v) is a 1-type.
For the same structure, let q(v) = {Ï†(v) âˆˆLA : M |= Ï†( 1

the formula v < 3 is in q(v), whereas v > 2 is not. For any LA-formula
Ïˆ(v), either M |= Ïˆ( 1

4. Realizing and Omitting Types
The latter example can be generalized to produce complete types in arbi-
trary structures. If M is any L-structure, A âŠ‚M, and a = (a1, . . . , an) âˆˆ
M n, let tpM(a/A) = {Ï†(v1, . . . , vn) âˆˆLA : M |= Ï†(a1, . . . , an)}. Then,

Deï¬nition 4.1.2 If p is an n-type over A, we say that a âˆˆM n realizes p if
M |= Ï†(a) for all Ï† âˆˆp. If p is not realized in M we say that M omits p.
In the examples given above, p(v) is not realized in M = (Q, <), whereas
clearly 1/2 realizes q(v). In fact, there are many realizations of q(v) in M.
Suppose that r is any rational number with 0 < r < 1. We can construct
an automorphism Ïƒ of M that ï¬xes every natural number but Ïƒ(1/2) =
r. Because Ïƒ ï¬xes all elements of A, Ïƒ is also an LA-automorphism. By
Theorem 1.1.10,
M |= Ï†(1/2) â‡”M |= Ï†(r).
Thus, r also realizes q(v).
In fact, the elements of Q that realize q(v) are exactly the rational num-
bers s such that 0 < s < 1. If s â‰¤0, then the formula 0 < v is in q(v) but
M |= Â¬(0 < s). Thus, s does not realize q(v). Similarly, no s â‰¥1 realizes
q(v).
The Compactness Theorem tells us that every type can be realized in an
elementary extension.

4.2
Omitting Types and Prime Models
The Compactness Theorem allows us to build models realizing types. It
is often also useful to build models that omit certain types. Let L be a
language and T an L-theory. For p an n-type consistent with T, we would
like to know whether there is M |= T omitting p. It is not hard to give a
necessary topological condition.
For T an L-theory, we let Sn(T) be the set of all complete n-types p such
that pâˆªT is satisï¬able. If T is complete and M |= T, then Sn(T) = SM
n (âˆ…).
In particular, Sn(T) is a totally disconnected compact topological space
with basic open sets

For p a complete type, p is isolated in Sn(T) if and only if {p} = [Ï†] for
some Ï†. We can extend this notion to possibly incomplete types.

is satisï¬able, and let p be an n-type. We say that Ï† isolates p if
T |= âˆ€v(Ï†(v) â†’Ïˆ(v))
for all Ïˆ âˆˆp.

formula.

The proof of the Omitting Types Theorem can be generalized to omit

Theorem 4.2.4 Let L be a countable language, and let T be an L-theory.

The assumption of countability of L is necessary in the Omitting Types

4.2 Omitting Types and Prime Models
129
Prime and Atomic Models
We use the Omitting Types Theorem to study small models of a com-
plete theory. For the remainder of this section, we will assume that L is a
countable language and T is a complete L-theory with inï¬nite models.
Deï¬nition 4.2.6 We say that M |= T is a prime model of T if whenever
N |= T there is an elementary embedding of M into N.
For example, let T =ACF0. If K |=ACF0, and F is the algebraic closure
of Q, then there is an embedding of F into K. Because ACF0 is model
complete this embedding is elementary. Thus, F is a prime model of ACF0.
Similarly, RCF has a prime model, the real closure of Q.

Prime Model Extensions of Ï‰-Stable Theories
We conclude this section by looking at a relative notion of prime models.
Suppose that M |= T and A âŠ†M. We say that M is prime over A
if whenever N |= T and f : A â†’N is partial elementary, there is an
elementary f âˆ—: M â†’N extending f.
We give three examples. Let L be any linear order. We build Lâˆ—|= DLO
prime over L as follows. If L has a least element a, add a copy of Q below a.
If L has a greatest element b, add a copy of Q above b. If c, d âˆˆL with c < d
but there are no elements of L between c and d, add a copy of Q between
c and d. We add no other new elements. It is easy to see that Lâˆ—|= DLO
and that if f : L â†’M |= DLO, then f extends to f âˆ—: Lâˆ—â†’M. Because
DLO has quantiï¬er elimination, it is model-complete and f âˆ—is elementary.
For ACF, if R is any integral domain and F is the algebraic closure of the
fraction ï¬eld of R, then F is prime over R and any embedding of R into an
algebraically closed ï¬eld K extends to F. Because ACF is model-complete,
this map is elementary. Similarly, if R is an ordered integral domain, then
the real closure of the fraction ï¬eld of R is a model of RCF prime over R.
In Exercise 4.5.26, we will give examples of theories without prime model
extensions.
There is one very natural class of theories with prime model extensions.
This class will play a very important role later in the book.

and let Îº be an inï¬nite cardinal. We say that T is Îº-stable if whenever
M |= T, A âŠ†M, and |A| = Îº, then |SM
n (A)| = Îº.

Curiously, theories with large countable models also have small countable

Corollary 4.3.8 i) If T has a countable saturated model, then T has a
prime model.
ii) If T has fewer than 2â„µ0 countable models, then T has a countable
saturated model and a prime model.
Proof
i) If T has a saturated model, then |Sn(T)| is countable for all n. By
Theorem 4.2.11, the isolated types are dense in Sn(T) for all n. Thus, by
Theorem 4.2.10, T has a prime model.
ii) It suï¬ƒces to show that Sn(T) is countable for all n < Ï‰. Suppose
not. By Theorem 4.2.11, if |Sn(T)| > â„µ0, then |Sn(T)| = 2â„µ0. Each n-type
must be realized in some countable model. Because each countable model
realizes only countably many n-types, if there are 2â„µ0 n-types, then there
must be 2â„µ0 nonisomorphic countable models.
We consider several examples.
Example 4.3.9 Dense Linear Orders
We will show that (Q, <) is saturated. Suppose A âŠ‚Q is ï¬nite. Suppose
that A = {a1, . . . , am} where a1 < . . . < am. By the analysis of types in
DLO given in Section 4.1, there are exactly 2m+1 types in S1(A). Each
type is isolated by one of the formulas v = ai, v < a0, ai < v < ai+1, or
am < v. Clearly, all of these types are realized in Q. Note that in this case
Q is both saturated and prime! In Section 4.4, we will see that this always
happens in â„µ0-categorical theories.
Example 4.3.10 Algebraically Closed Fields
Fix p prime or 0. Let k be Fp if p > 0 and Q if p = 0. Because Sn(ACFp) is in
bijection with Spec(k[X1, . . . , Xn]), by Corollary 4.1.18, |Sn(ACFp)| = â„µ0.
Thus, there is a countable saturated model of ACFp.
Let qn be the type corresponding to the 0 ideal in k[X1, . . . , Xn]. If
a1, . . . , an realizes qn, then a1, . . . , an are algebraically independent over
k. Thus, any saturated model has inï¬nite transcendence degree. It follows

closed ï¬eld of characteristic p and transcendence degree â„µ0.

Homogeneous and Universal Models
Although prime models elementarily embed into all models of T, saturated
models embed all small models.
Deï¬nition 4.3.16 We say that M |= T is Îº-universal if for all N |= T
with |N| < Îº there is an elementary embedding of N into M.
We say that M is universal if it is |M|+-universal.

Vaughtâ€™s Two-Cardinal Theorem

will be useful in Chapter 6. If M is an L-structure and Ï†(v1, . . . , vn) is an

Deï¬nition 4.3.33
Let Îº > Î» â‰¥â„µ0. We say that an L-theory T has a

and |Ï†(M)| = Î».
(Îº, Î»)-models are an obstruction to Îº-categoricity. If T is a theory in a
countable language with inï¬nite models, then an easy compactness argu-

Theorem 4.3.34 If T has a (Îº, Î»)-model where Îº > Î» â‰¥â„µ0, then T has
an (â„µ1, â„µ0)-model.

Deï¬nition 4.3.35 We say that (N, M) is a Vaughtian pair of models of
T if M â‰ºN, M Ì¸= N, and there is an LM-formula Ï† such that Ï†(M) is

deï¬nes an inï¬nite set containing no elements of N \ M.

4.4
The Number of Countable Models
Throughout this section, T will be a complete theory in a countable lan-
guage with inï¬nite models.
For any inï¬nite cardinal Îº, we let I(T, Îº) be the number of nonisomorphic
models of T of cardinality Îº. In this section, we will look at the possible
values of I(T, â„µ0). We have already considered a number of examples.
â€¢ I(DLO,â„µ0) = 1.
â€¢ In Exercise 2.5.28, we gave examples of Tn where I(Tn, â„µ0) = n for
n = 3, 4, . . . ,.
â€¢ I(ACFp, â„µ0) = â„µ0.
â€¢ I(RCF, â„µ0) = I(Th(N), â„µ0) = 2â„µ0.
Because there are at most 2â„µ0 nonisomorphic countable models of T,
there are two natural questions:
Can we have I(T, â„µ0) = 2?
Can we have â„µ0 < I(T, â„µ0) < 2â„µ0?
Surprisingly, Vaught answered the ï¬rst question negatively. If the Con-
tinuum Hypothesis is true, then the second question has a trivial negative
answer. Vaught conjectured that the answer is negative even when the
Continuum Hypothesis fails. This remains one of the deep open questions
of model theory. Although Vaughtâ€™s Conjecture has been proved for some
special classes of theories (for example, Shelah [93] proved Vaughtâ€™s Con-
jecture for Ï‰-stable theories), the best general result is Morleyâ€™s theorem
that if I(T, â„µ0) > â„µ1, then I(T, â„µ0) = 2â„µ0.

We begin by taking a closer look at â„µ0-categorical theories. In particular,
we show how to recognize â„µ0-categoricity by looking at the type space.

I(T, â„µ0) > â„µ1, then I(T, â„µ0) = 2â„µ0.
Proof For any countable fragment F, Sn(F, T) is analytic. Thus, by The-

Remarks
The Omitting Types Theorem is due to Henkin and Orey , each of whom
used it to prove the Completeness Theorem for Ï‰-logic. Theorem 4.2.5
is due to MacDowell and Specker. Their proof uses an ultraproduct con-
struction and works for uncountable models as well. See [51] Â§8.2 for further
results on end extensions of models of arithmetic.

172
4. Realizing and Omitting Types
The results on prime models, atomic models, and countable saturated
models are due to Vaught and appear in [99], one of the most elegant
papers in model theory. Theorem 4.3.23 is due to Keisler, and the other
basic results on saturated and homogeneous models are due to Morley and
Vaught.
Recursively saturated models were introduced by Barwise and Schlipf.
Many results that can be proved using saturated models have elegant proofs
using recursively saturated models (see [22] Â§2.4 or [53]). Friedman showed
the weak recursive saturation of nonstandard models of arithmetic and used
it to prove the following result (see [51] Â§12.1).
Theorem 4.5.47 If M is a countable nonstandard model of Peano arith-
metic, then there is I a proper initial segment of M with I âˆ¼= M.
The â„µ1-saturation of ultraproducts is due to Keisler (see [22] Â§6.1 for
generalizations).
Morley introduced Ï‰-stable theories in his proof of Theorem 6.1.1. He
also proved that Ï‰-stable theories have prime model extensions. Shelah
showed that there are three possibilities for {Îº â‰¥â„µ0 : T is Îº-stable}.
Theorem 4.5.48 If T is a complete theory in a countable language, then
one of the following holds:
i) there are no cardinals Îº such that T is Îº-stable,
ii) T is Îº-stable for all Îº â‰¥2â„µ0,
iii) T is Îº-stable if and only if Îºâ„µ0 = Îº.
A proof of this theorem can be found in [7], [18] or [76]. If i) holds, we
say that T is unstable; otherwise, we say that T is stable.
If ii) holds,
we say that T is superstable. By Theorem 4.2.18, every Ï‰-stable theory is
superstable. In Exercise 4.5.22, we gave an example of a superstable theory
that is not Ï‰-stable and a stable theory that is not superstable.
The saturated model test for quantiï¬er elimination is due to Blum, who
also axiomatized the theory of DCF, proved that DCF is Ï‰-stable, and
deduced from that the existence of diï¬€erential closures.
In Theorem 5.2.15, we will examine another two-cardinal result. There
are many interesting two cardinal questions, but most can not be answered
in ZFC. The following Theorem gives several interesting examples.
Theorem 4.5.49 Let L be a countable language and T an L-theory.
i) Assume that V = L.3 If Îº > Î» â‰¥â„µ0 and T has a (Îº, Î»)-model, then T
has a (Âµ+, Âµ)-model for all inï¬nite cardinals Âµ.
ii) Assume that V = L. If T has a (Îº++, Îº)-model for some inï¬nite
cardinal Îº, then T has a (Î»++, Î»)-model for all inï¬nite cardinals Î».
3V = L is GÂ¨odelâ€™s Axiom of Constructibility asserting that all sets are constructible
(see [57] or [47]).

4.5 Exercises and Remarks
173
iii) If ZFC is consistent, then it is consistent with ZFC that there is a
countable theory with an (â„µ1, â„µ0)-model but no (â„µ2, â„µ1)-model.
The ï¬rst result was proved by Chang (see [22] 7.2.7) for regular Âµ under
the weaker assumption that the Generalized Continuum Hypothesis holds.
The general case is due to Jensen who also proved the second result (see
[27] Â§VIII). The third result is due to Mitchell and Silver (see [47] Â§29).
The characterization of â„µ0-categorical theories was proved independently
by Ryll-Nardzewski, Engler, and Svenonius.
Although Vaughtâ€™s Conjecture is open for arbitrary theories, we do know
that it holds for several interesting classes of theories.
Theorem 4.5.50 Vaughtâ€™s Conjecture holds for:
i) (Shelah [93]) Ï‰-stable theories;
ii) (Buechler [19]) superstable theories of ï¬nite U-rank;
iii) (Mayer [69]) o-minimal theories;
iv) (Miller) theories of linear orders with unary predicates;
v) (Steel [98]) theories of trees.
See [100] for more on iv) and v).
Theorem 4.4.16 also follows from another powerful theorem in descriptive
set theory. Consider the equivalence relation on D(L, T) given by fEg if
and only if Mf âˆ¼= Mg. It is easy to argue that E is an analytic subset of
D(L, T)Ã—D(L, T). Burgess (see, for example, [98]) proved that any analytic
equivalence relation on a Borel subset of 2Ï‰ with at least â„µ2 classes has 2â„µ0
classes.
If Ï† is an LÏ‰1,Ï‰-sentence, we can ask about the number of nonisomorphic
countable models of Ï†. Burgessâ€™ Theorem shows that if there are at least
â„µ2 nonisomorphic models, then there are 2â„µ0, but it is unknown whether
there can be an LÏ‰1,Ï‰-sentence with exactly â„µ1 < 2â„µ0 models.
Questions around Vaughtâ€™s Conjecture can be reformulated in a way
that does not involve any model theory. We say that a topological space
X is Polish if it is a complete separable metric space. Suppose that G is
a Polish topological group and G acts continuously on a Borel subset X
of a Polish space X. For example, X could be D(L, T) and G could be
the group of permutations of Ï‰ topologized by taking subbasic open sets
Nn,m = {f : f(n) = m}. The Topological Vaught Conjecture asserts that
if G has uncountably many orbits on X, then G has 2â„µ0 orbits. See [9] for
more on this topic.

5.2
Order Indiscernibles
Let M be an L-structure.
Deï¬nition 5.2.1 Let I be an inï¬nite set and suppose that X = {xi : i âˆˆI}
is a set of distinct elements of M. We say that X is an indiscernible set if
whenever i1, . . . , im and j1, . . . , jm are two sequences of m distinct elements
of I, then M |= Ï†(xi1, . . . , xim) â†”Ï†(xj1, . . . , xjm).
For example, suppose that F is an algebraically closed ï¬eld of inï¬nite
transcendence degree and x1, x2, . . . is an inï¬nite algebraically independent
set. For any two sequence i1, . . . , im, j1, . . . , jm as above, there is an au-
tomorphism Ïƒ of F with Ïƒ(xik) = xjk for k = 1, . . . , m. It follows that
x1, x2, . . . is an inï¬nite set of indiscernibles.
Unfortunately, many structures have no inï¬nite sets of indiscernibles. For
example if (A, <) is an inï¬nite linear order, then because we cannot have
a < b and b < a there is no set of indiscernibles of size 2. Remarkably, this
is the only obstruction.

5.2 Order Indiscernibles
179
Deï¬nition 5.2.2 Let (I, <) be an ordered set, and let (xi : i âˆˆI) be a
sequence of distinct elements of M. We say that (xi : i âˆˆI) is a sequence
of order indiscernibles if whenever i1 < i2 < . . . < im and j1 < . . . <
jm are two increasing sequences from I, then M |= Ï†(xi1, . . . , xim) â†”
Ï†(xj1, . . . , xjm).
For example, in (Q, <), by quantiï¬er elimination, if x1 < . . . < xm and
y1 < . . . < ym, then Q |= Ï†(x) â†”Ï†(y) for all Ï†. Thus Q, itself is a sequence
of order indiscernibles.
We can always ï¬nd models with inï¬nite sequences of order indiscernibles.

Ehrenfeuchtâ€“Mostowski Models
Suppose that our theory has built-in Skolem functions. Then, when we
have a model containing an inï¬nite sequence of order indiscernibles, we can
form the elementary submodel generated by the indiscernibles. We can use
properties of the indiscernible set to deduce properties of the elementary
submodel. For example, automorphisms of the indiscernibles will induce
automorphisms of the elementary submodel. If T does not have built in
Skolem functions, we will still get interesting information when we study
skolemizations of T.
Let T be an L-theory. By Lemma 2.3.6 we can ï¬nd Lâˆ—âŠ‡L, and T âˆ—âŠ‡T

of T, we can interpret the symbols of Lâˆ—such that M |= T âˆ—. Note that if
I is a sequence of order indiscernibles for Lâˆ—, then I is also a sequence of
order indiscernibles for L.
If M |= T âˆ—and X âŠ†M, let H(X) be the Lâˆ—-substructure of M generated
by X. We call H(X) the Skolem hull of X. Because X has built in Skolem
functions, H(X) â‰ºM. Models built as Skolem hulls of sequences of order
indiscernibles are called Ehrenfeuchtâ€“Mostowski models.
If I is an inï¬nite set of order indiscernibles, then order-preserving per-
mutations of I induce automorphisms of H(I).

Applications of ErdÂ¨osâ€“Rado
So far, we have tried to build interesting models by controlling the order
type of the sequence of indiscernibles. There are other constructions where
we instead control tp(I). This is more diï¬ƒcult and often requires more
complicated combinatorics.
Corollary 5.2.8 showed that if we can omit a type in a model of T âˆ—
containing an inï¬nite sequence of order indiscernibles, then we can omit
the type in arbitrarily large models, but it is nontrivial to ï¬nd models
with indiscernibles omitting a type. The ErdÂ¨osâ€“Rado Theorem provides
one method for building such models.

5.3 A Many-Models Theorem
189
5.3
A Many-Models Theorem
In this section, we give a taste of one of the main applications of indis-
cernibles. Let T be a complete theory in a countable language with inï¬nite
models. We know that T has at most 2Îº nonisomorphic models of cardinal-
ity Îº. In a series of results, Shelah forged dividing lines between theories
where there is a good structure theory for the models and theories where
there is none. One of the main ideas is that theories with no good structure
theory have 2Îº nonisomorphic models of size Îº for all Îº â‰¥â„µ1.
Deï¬nition 5.3.1 We say that T is stable if it is Î»-stable for some Î» â‰¥â„µ0;
otherwise, we say T is unstable.
Stability is the ï¬rst dividing line for structure vs. nonstructure. Shelah
proved that if T is unstable, then T has 2Îº nonisomorphic models of car-
dinality Îº for all Îº â‰¥â„µ0. Indeed, he showed that unless T is Îº-stable for
all Îº â‰¥2â„µ0, then T has the maximal number of nonisomorphic models for
each uncountable cardinal Îº. We will prove a special case of the ï¬rst result.
Assumptions For the rest of this section, we will make the following as-
sumptions:
â€¢ T is a complete theory in a countable language L with inï¬nite models;
â€¢ there is a binary relation symbol < in the language;
â€¢ there is M |= T containing an inï¬nite set linearly ordered by <.
These simplifying assumptions are not particularly strong. In fact, if T
is any unstable theory, we can ï¬nd M |= T and I âŠ‚M k an inï¬nite set
that is linearly ordered by some L-formula Ï†(x, y). The arguments we will
give can easily be modiï¬ed to work in the general unstable context.
Theorem 5.3.2 If Îº â‰¥â„µ1, then there are 2Îº nonisomorphic models of T.
To simplify the combinatorics of the proof, we will prove this only for
regular Îº > â„µ1. The proof for Îº = â„µ1 is a bit harder. The proof for Îº singular
is much more involved. We begin with some results from combinatorial set
theory that will be used in the proof.
