5.4
An Independence Result in Arithmetic
GÂ¨odelâ€™s famous Incompleteness Theorem asserts that there are sentences Ï†
in the language of arithmetic such that Ï† is true in the natural numbers
but unprovable from the Peano Axioms for arithmetic. Indeed, for any con-
sistent recursive extension T of Peano arithmetic, we can ï¬nd a sentence
that is independent from T. The original independent sentences were self-
referential sentences that asserted their own unprovability or metamathe-
matical sentences asserting the consistency of the theory. People wondered

196
5. Indiscernibles
whether the independent statements could be made more â€œmathematical.â€
In the late 1970s, Paris and Harrington [73] showed that a slight variant
of the ï¬nite version of Ramseyâ€™s Theorem is true but unprovable in Peano
arithmetic. The proof is an interesting application of indiscernibles.
We begin with the combinatorial statement.
Theorem 5.4.1 (Parisâ€“Harrington Principle) For all natural num-
bers n, k, m, there is a number l such that if f : [l]n â†’k, then there is
Y âŠ†l such that Y is homogeneous for f, |Y | â‰¥m, and if y0 is the least
element of Y , then |Y | â‰¥y0.
Proof We argue as in the proof of the ï¬nite version of Ramseyâ€™s Theorem.
Suppose that there is no such l. For l < Ï‰, let Tl = {f : [{0, . . . , l âˆ’1}]n â†’
k : there is no Y homogeneous for f with |Y | â‰¥m, min Y }. Clearly, each
Tl is ï¬nite, and if f âˆˆTl+1 there is a unique g âˆˆTl such that g âŠ‚f. Thus,
if we order T =  Tl by inclusion, we get a ï¬nite branching tree. Because
each Tl is nonempty, T is an inï¬nite ï¬nite branching tree and by KÂ¨onigâ€™s
Lemma there is f0 âŠ‚f1 âŠ‚f2 âŠ‚. . . with fi âˆˆTi.
Let f =  fi. Then f : [N]n â†’k. By Ramseyâ€™s Theorem, there is an
inï¬nite X âŠ†N homogeneous for f. Let x1 be the least element of X,
and choose s â‰¥x1, m. Let x1, . . . , xl be the ï¬rst l-elements of X and let
l > xl. Then, Y = {x1, . . . , xl} is homogeneous for fs and |Y | â‰¥m, min Y ,
a contradiction.
Although the proof above is only a minor variant of the proof of the
ï¬nite version of Ramseyâ€™s Theorem, the use of the inï¬nite version of Ram-
seyâ€™s Theorem is in this case unavoidable. We will show that the Parisâ€“
Harrington Principle cannot be proved in Peano arithmetic. The approach
we give here is due to Kanamori and McAloon [49].
Deï¬nition 5.4.2 Let X âŠ†Ï‰. We say that f : [X]n â†’Ï‰ is regressive if
f(A) < min A for all A âˆˆ[X]n. We say that Y âŠ†X is min-homogeneous
for f, if whenever A, B âˆˆ[Y ]n and min A = min B, then f(A) = f(B).
If a < b, we let (a, b) and [a, b] denote {x : a < x < b} and {x : a â‰¤x â‰¤
b}, respectively.
We will consider the combinatorial principle.
(âˆ—) For all c, m, n, k, there is d such that if f1, . . . , fk : [d]n â†’d are
regressive, then there is Y âŠ†[c, d] such that |Y | â‰¥m and Y is min-

We will show that (âˆ—) is true but not provable in Peano arithmetic. We
begin by giving a ï¬nite combinatorial proof that (âˆ—) follows from the Parisâ€“
Harrington Principle. This proof can be formalized in Peano arithmetic.
This tells us that not only is (âˆ—) true but also if it is not provable in Peano
arithmetic, then neither is the Paris-Harrington Principle.

To prove the independence of the Parisâ€“Harrington Principle from Peano
arithmetic, we will assume familiarity with formalizing ï¬nite combinatorics

5.5 Exercises and Remarks
205
Remarks
Ehrenfeucht and Mostowski introduced indiscernibles and showed that
Skolem hulls of models generated by indiscernibles could be used to build
models with many automorphisms. Morley showed that indiscernibles could
be used to build large models realizing few types and also showed that the
ErdÂ¨osâ€“Rado Theorem could be used to build models with indiscernibles
omitting a type. Theorem 2.11 is due to Vaught.
Shelah has proved much ï¬ner many-models theorems than the one
sketched in Section 5.3. For example, if T is unstable, then, for any uncount-
able Îº, we can ï¬nd a family of 2Îº mutually nonelementarily embeddable
models of T that are all Lâˆ,Ï‰-equivalent. The simpliï¬ed proof in Section 5.3
is from Poizatâ€™s review [85] of Shelah. Proofs of the many-models theorems
that we mentioned can be found in Shelah [92]. Hodges [40] contains the
proof that unstable theories have 2Îº nonisomorphic models of cardinality Îº
for all regular uncountable Îº (in particular, this includes the case Îº = â„µ1,
which we ignored). Baldwin [7] has the proof that stable unsuperstable
theories have the maximum number of models in uncountable cardinals in
regular cardinals.
The proof of the independence of the Parisâ€“Harrington Principle given
is Section 5.4 is from Kanamori and McAloon [49]. The growth analysis of
Exercise 5.5.13 is just the tip of the iceberg because we have only really used
Parisâ€“Harrington for partitions of pairs of sets. The sequence f0, f1, . . . can
be extended to fÎ± for Î± < Ïµ0. For example, fÏ‰(n) = fn(n). Any recursive
function that we can prove total in Peano arithmetic is majorized by fÎ±
for some Î± < Ïµ0. These classic results of proof theory are surveyed in [33].
Let G(n) be the least number m such that whenever g : [m]n â†’n there
is X âŠ‚m homogeneous for g with |X| > n + 1, min X. By the Parisâ€“
Harrington Principle, G is a total recursive function, but G majorizes fÎ±
for all Î± < Ïµ0. This analysis was done by Ketonen and Solovay and is
explained well in [36]. Kanamori and McAloon do a similar analysis for
their combinatorial principle.
Indiscernibles also play a central role in the set-theoretic investigation of
large cardinals. Rowbottom showed that if there is a measurable cardinal,
then there are indiscernibles for GÂ¨odelâ€™s constructible universe. This idea
was developed much further by Silver. These results can be found in [47]
Â§29â€“30 or [48] Â§9.

6
Ï‰-Stable Theories
6.1
Uncountably Categorical Theories
Throughout this chapter, T will be a complete theory in a countable lan-
guage with inï¬nite models.
We say that T is uncountably categorical if it is Îº-categorical for some
uncountable Îº. We have already seen several examples of uncountably cat-
egorical theories. For example, the theory of algebraically closed ï¬elds of
a ï¬xed characteristic, the theory of (Z, s), and the theory of torsion-free
divisible Abelian groups are Îº-categorical for all uncountable Îº but not
â„µ0-categorical. On the other hand, the theory of an inï¬nite Abelian group
where every element has order 2 is Îº-categorical for all inï¬nite cardinals.
Every uncountably categorical theory that we have examined is actu-
ally Îº-categorical for all uncountable Îº. Morley proved that this is true
for all countable uncountably categorical theories. Morleyâ€™s proof was the
beginning of modern model theory.
Theorem 6.1.1 (Categoricity Theorem) If T is Îº-categorical for some
uncountable Îº, then T is Îº-categorical for every uncountable Îº.
In Theorems 5.2.10 and 5.2.11, we proved two important facts about
uncountably categorical theories.
â€¢ If Îº â‰¥â„µ1 and T is Îº-categorical, then T is Ï‰-stable.
â€¢ If Îº â‰¥â„µ1 and T is Îº-categorical, then T has no Vaughtian pairs.
Baldwin and Lachlan showed that, conversely, if T is Ï‰-stable with no
Vaughtian pairs, then T is Îº-categorical for all uncountable cardinals Îº.

If M is an L-structure and Ï†(v) is an LM-formula, we will let Ï†(M) denote

Deï¬nition 6.1.2 Let M be an L-structure and let D âŠ†M n be an inï¬nite

We say that a theory T is strongly minimal if the formula v = v is

We have already seen several examples of strongly minimal theories.

theories (see Corollaries 3.1.11 and 3.2.9).

The Categoricity Theorem
Our proof of categoricity will follow the argument in Theorem 6.1.11. We
can ï¬nd a strongly minimal formula Ï†(v) over the prime model. If M and
N are models of T of the same uncountable cardinality, then we can ï¬nd
a partial elementary bijection between Ï†(M) and Ï†(N). The next lemma
allows us to extend this to an isomorphism between M and N.
Lemma 6.1.17 If T has no Vaughtian pairs, M |= T, and X âŠ†M n is
inï¬nite and deï¬nable, then no proper elementary submodel of M contains
X. If, in addition, T is Ï‰-stable, then M is prime over X.
Proof
Let Ï†(v) deï¬ne X. If N is a proper elementary submodel of M
containing X, then X = Ï†(M) = Ï†(N) and (M, N) is a Vaughtian pair.
If T is Ï‰-stable, then by Theorem 4.2.20, there is N â‰ºM, a prime model
over X. Because T has no Vaughtian pairs, we must have N = M, so M
is prime over X.

6.2
Morley Rank
Throughout this chapter, T is a complete theory with inï¬nite models.
In this section, we will develop Morley rank, one of the most impor-
tant tools for analyzing Ï‰-stable theories. Morley rank provides a notion
of â€œdimension.â€ We begin with an illustration from linear algebra. Suppose
that K is an inï¬nite ï¬eld and V âŠ†Kn is an m-dimensional vector space.
Suppose that f is a linear function that is not constant on V . For a âˆˆK,
let Va = {x âˆˆV : f(x) = a}. Then, {Va : a âˆˆK} is an inï¬nite family
of (m âˆ’1)-dimensional aï¬ƒne subsets of V . Morley rank is an attempt to
generalize this property of dimension. The basic idea is that if a deï¬nable
set X contains inï¬nitely many pairwise disjoint sets of dimension m, then
X should have dimension at least m + 1. To make this precise, we must
allow arbitrary ordinals to occur as dimensions.

formula. We will deï¬ne RMM(Ï†) the, Morley rank of Ï† in M. First, we

The Monster Model
The deï¬nition we just gave of Morley rank is rather awkward because
even if a formula has parameters from M |= T we need to work in an
â„µ0-saturated elementary extension to calculate the Morley rank. Then, to
show that Morley rank is well-deï¬ned, we must show that our calculation
did not depend on our choice of â„µ0-saturated model. Arguments such as
this come up very frequently and tend to be both routine and repetitive. To
simplify proofs, we will frequently adopt the expository device of assuming
that we are working in a ï¬xed, very large, saturated model of T.
Let M |= T be saturated of cardinality Îº, where Îº is â€œvery large.â€ We
call M the monster model of T. If M |= T and |M| â‰¤Îº, then by Lemma

6.2 Morley Rank
219
4.3.17 there is an elementary embedding of M into M. Moreover, if M â‰ºM,
f : M â†’N is elementary, and |N| < Îº, we can ï¬nd j : N â†’M elementary
such that j|M is the identity. Thus, if we focus attention on models of T
of cardinality less than Îº, we can view all models as elementary submodels
of M.
There are several problems with this approach. First, we really want
to prove theorems about all models of T, not just the small ones. But
if there are arbitrarily large saturated models of T, then we can prove
something about all models of T by proving it for submodels of larger and
larger monster models. Second, and more problematic, for general theories
T there may not be any saturated models. For our purposes, this is not a
serious problem because, for the remainder of this text, we will be focusing
on Ï‰-stable theories and, by Theorem 4.3.15, if T is Ï‰-stable, there are
saturated models of T of cardinality Îº for each regular cardinal Îº. If we
were considering arbitrary theories, we could get around this by making
some extra set-theoretic assumptions. For example, we could assume that
for all cardinals Î» there is a strongly inaccessible cardinal Îº > Î». Then, by
Corollary 4.3.14, there are arbitrarily large saturated models.
We will tacitly assume that T has arbitrarily large saturated models, and
thus we can prove theorems about all models of T by proving theorems
about elementary submodels of saturated models.1 We will only use this
assumption in arguments where, by careful bookkeeping as in the proofs
above, we could avoid it.
For the remainder of the chapter, we make the following assumptions:
â€¢ M is a large saturated model of T;
â€¢ all M |= T that we consider are elementary submodels of M and
|M| < |M|;
â€¢ all sets A of parameters that we consider are subsets of M with |A| < M;
â€¢ if Ï†(v, a) is a formula with parameters, we assume a âˆˆM;
â€¢ we write tp(a/A) for tpM(a/A) and Sn(A) for SM
n (A).
Note that if a âˆˆM, then, because M â‰ºM, M |= Ï†(a) if and only if
M |= Ï†(a). We will say that Ï†(a) holds if M |= Ï†(a).
Because M is saturated, if A âŠ‚M and p âˆˆSn(A), then p is realized in
M. Moreover, if f : A â†’M is a partial elementary map, then f extends to
an automorphism of M.
We could deï¬ne Morley rank referring only to the monster model. The
Morley rank of an LM-formula is inductively deï¬ned as follows:
RM(Ï†) â‰¥0 if and only if Ï†(M) is nonempty;
RM(Ï†) â‰¥Î± + 1 if and only if there are LM-formulas Ïˆ1, Ïˆ2, . . . such
that Ïˆ1(M), Ïˆ2(M), . . . is an inï¬nite sequence of pairwise disjoint subsets
of Ï†(M) and RM(Ïˆi) â‰¥Î± for each i;
1There are other approaches to the monster model, which we discuss in the remarks
at the end of this chapter.

In particular, this shows that Morley rank is preserved by deï¬nable bi-

Corollary 6.2.18 Suppose that T is Ï‰-stable, M |= T, X âŠ†Mn, Y âŠ†Mm
are deï¬nable, and f : X â†’Y is a deï¬nable ï¬nite-to-one function from X
onto Y . Then RM(X) = RM(Y ).
Proof
Let A âŠ‚M such that X, Y and f are deï¬nable over A. Suppose
that f(a) = b. Then, b is deï¬nable over A, a and, because f is ï¬nite-to-one,
a is algebraic over A, b. By Lemma 6.2.17
RM(a/A) = RM(a, b/A) = RM(b/A).
If a âˆˆX such that RM(a/A) = RM(X), then
RM(Y ) â‰¥RM(f(a)/A) = RM(a/A) = RM(X).
On the other hand, if b âˆˆY such that RM(b/A) = RM(Y ), then, because
f is surjective, there is a âˆˆX such that f(a) = b and
RM(X) â‰¥RM(a/A) = RM(b/A) = RM(Y ).
Hence RM(X) = RM(Y ).

Morley Rank in Algebraically Closed Fields
For algebraically closed ï¬elds, we will show that Morley rank is equal to
the classical notion of dimension from algebraic geometry.
Deï¬nition 6.2.21 Let K be an algebraically closed ï¬eld. Let V âŠ†Kn be
an irreducible algebraic variety. Let I(V ) be the prime ideal of polynomials
in K[X1, . . . , Xn] vanishing on V . The Krull dimension of V is the largest
number m such that there is a chain of prime ideals
I(V ) = P0 âŠ‚P1 âŠ‚P2 âŠ‚. . . âŠ‚Pm âŠ‚K[X1, . . . , Xn].
If V has Krull dimension 0, then I(V ) is a maximal ideal. Because K is
algebraically closed, I(V ) is the ideal generated by X1 âˆ’a1, . . . , Xn âˆ’an
for some a1, . . . , an âˆˆK and V is the single point {a}.
There are many alternative characterizations of dimension. For example,
the Krull dimension is the minimum of the vector space dimension of the
tangent space to V at points p âˆˆV . If K = C and V is smooth, then
the Krull dimension is equal to the dimension of V as a complex man-
ifold. We will use another characterization of dimension. If V âŠ†Kn is
an algebraic variety, the function ï¬eld of V is K(V ), the fraction ï¬eld of
K[X1, . . . , Xn]/I(V ).
Theorem 6.2.22 If K is an algebraically closed ï¬eld and V âŠ†Kn is an
irreducible variety, then the Krull dimension of V is equal to the transcen-
dence degree of the function ï¬eld K(V ) over K.
Proof See [6] Â§11.
It follows that in algebraically closed ï¬elds, the Morley rank of a variety
is equal to the Krull dimension.
Corollary 6.2.23 If K is an algebraically closed ï¬eld and V âŠ†Kn is an
irreducible variety, then RM(V ) is equal to the Krull dimension of V .
Proof We prove this by induction on the Krull dimension of V . If V has
Krull dimension 0, then V is a point and RM(V ) = 0.
Suppose that V has Krull dimension k > 0. Let Ï†(v) be the LK-formula
deï¬ning V . If a âˆˆÏ†(M), let Va âŠ†V be
{x âˆˆKn : f(a) = 0 â†’f(x) = 0 for all f âˆˆK[X]}

6.4
Uniqueness of Prime Model Extensions
Throughout this section T will be a complete theory in a countable language
with inï¬nite models.
In Theorem 4.2.20 we proved the existence of prime model extensions for
Ï‰-stable theories. In this section, we will prove that these extensions are
unique up to isomorphism.
Constructible Models
Deï¬nition 6.4.1 Let M be an L-structure and let A âŠ†M. Let Î´ be an
ordinal and (aÎ± : Î± < Î´) be a sequence of elements from M. Let AÎ± =

is isolated for all Î± < Î´.
We say that B âŠ†M is constructible over A if there is a construction
(aÎ± : Î± < Î´) such that B = A âˆª{aÎ± : Î± < Î´}. We say that a model M is
constructible over A if M is constructible over A.
Note that if (aÎ± : Î± < Î´) is a construction over A, then it is also a
construction over AÎ± for all Î± < Î´.
Our proof of Theorem 4.2.20 can be broken into the following two asser-
tions about constructible models.
Lemma 6.4.2 i) If T is Ï‰-stable, M |= T, and A âŠ‚M, there is N â‰ºM
constructible over A.

We will prove the uniqueness of prime model extensions in two steps.

unique for any theory. We will then prove that in Ï‰-stable theories all prime
model extensions are constructible.

Prime Models of Ï‰-Stable Theories
It remains to show that if T is Ï‰-stable, then any prime model extension is
constructible. We need one lemma relating forking and isolation in Ï‰-stable
theories. This lemma is a special case of the Open Mapping Theorem (see
Exercise 6.6.30).

Theorem 6.4.8 (Uniqueness of Prime Models) Suppose that T is Ï‰-
stable, A âŠ†M, M0 â‰ºM, M1 â‰ºM, and M0 and M1 are prime models
over A. The identity map on A extends to an isomorphism between M0
and M1.
Proof
By Lemma 6.4.2, there is N â‰ºM, a constructible model over A.
Because each Mi is prime over A, we can ï¬nd an elementary embedding
of Mi into N. By the previous lemma, each Mi is constructible. Thus,
by the uniqueness of constructible models, there is f : M0 â†’M1, an
isomorphism ï¬xing A pointwise.
Diï¬€erential Closures
We give one application of the uniqueness of prime models.
Deï¬nition 6.4.9 Let K and k be diï¬€erential ï¬elds with k âŠ†K. We say

is an elementary embedding. Thus, a diï¬€erential closure of k is a model of
DCF prime over k. In Exercise 4.5.43, we argued that DCF is Ï‰-stable.
Theorem 6.4.10 Let k be a diï¬€erential ï¬eld. There is K âŠ‡k a diï¬€erential

for all a âˆˆK.
Proof Because DCF is Ï‰-stable and a diï¬€erential closure of k is a prime

Because the diï¬€erential closure is a prime model over k, every a âˆˆK
realizes an isolated type over k.
6.5
Morley Sequences
In Theorem 4.3.15 we proved that if T is Ï‰-stable and Îº is a regular cardinal,
then T has a saturated model of cardinality Îº. In this section we will extend

Throughout this section T will be a complete Ï‰-stable theory in a count-

Remarks
Morley [71] introduced ranks in his proof of the Categoricity Theorem. He
originally deï¬ned rank using the Cantorâ€“Bendixson derivative as discussed
in Exercise 6.6.19.
There are several alternative approaches to the monster model M. Ziegler
[104] views the monster model as a proper classâ€”rather than a setâ€”
containing all set models of T. Hodges [40] deï¬nes a notion of Îº-big models
such that if M is Îº-big, then M is Îº-saturated, and whenever A, B âŠ‚M,
|A|, |B| < Îº, and f : A â†’B is elementary, then f extends to an automor-
phism of M. He shows that if Îº is a regular cardinal greater that |L| + â„µ0,
then there is a Îº-big model M with |M| â‰¤Îº<Îº. If we are considering
models of cardinality less than Îº, we can use a Îº-big model as the monster
model.
All of Section 6.3 is due to Shelah. Although we have only deï¬ned forking
and independence for Ï‰-stable theories, these concepts can be deï¬ned for
arbitrary stable theories so that most of the results of Section 6.3 hold. One
notable exception is that, in a stable theory, if p âˆˆSn(A), then there is a
countable A0 âŠ†A such that p does not fork over A but p might fork over
every ï¬nite subset of A. There are a number of good references on stable
theoriesâ€”for example, [7], [18] or [75]. Recently, many of these ideas have
also been generalized to simple unstable theories (see, for example, [54]).
The notion of U-rank from Exercise 6.6.25 was introduced by Lascar. If
T is superstable, then RU(p) < âˆfor all p âˆˆSn(A). Although U-rank is
not quite as natural as Morley rank, it has some properties that make it

The uniqueness of constructible models is an unpublished result of
Ressayre.
In Exercise 4.5.28, we argued that we have prime model extensions in

The uniqueness of prime models is due to Shelah. The proof of Lemma
6.4.7 can easily be generalized to stable theories, although stable theories
need not have prime model extensions.
Blum [11] showed that DCF is Ï‰-stable and stability to show the ex-
istence and uniqueness of diï¬€erential closures. Kolchin [56] later gave an
algebraic proof. Kolchin, Rosenlicht, and Shelah gave independent proofs
that diï¬€erential closures need not be minimal. For more on diï¬€erentially
closed ï¬elds, see [65].
Morley introduced Morley sequences and proved Theorem 6.5.5. Theo-
rem 6.5.4 is due to Harnik and Shelah.

7
Ï‰-Stable Groups
7.1
The Descending Chain Condition
By an Ï‰-stable group, we mean an Ï‰-stable structure (G, Â·, 1, . . .) where
(G, Â·, 1) is a group. We will say that G is a group of ï¬nite Morley rank if
(G, Â·, 1, . . .) is Ï‰-stable with RM(G) < Ï‰.
We have already encountered several simple examples. Of course, all
ï¬nite groups are Ï‰-stable, but we will focus on inï¬nite groups. If G is a
torsion-free divisible Abelian group, then Th(G) is â„µ1-categorical and hence
Ï‰-stable. If p is prime, then an inï¬nite-dimensional vector space over Fp is
categorical in every inï¬nite cardinal and hence Ï‰-stable.
If M is Ï‰-stable and (G, Â·, 1, . . .) is interpretable in M, then G is Ï‰-stable.
In particular, any group interpretable in an algebraically closed ï¬eld is Ï‰-
stable. For example, the multiplicative group of an algebraically closed ï¬eld
is Ï‰-stable. We can go much further on these lines.
Algebraic Groups
Let K be an algebraically closed ï¬eld and let GLn(K) be the group of
invertible n Ã— n matrices over K. If A is an n Ã— n matrix over K, we
naturally think of A as an element of Kn2 and we can identify GLn(K) with
the Zariski closed set V = {(A, w) âˆˆKn2+1 : w det(A) = 1}. We can deï¬ne
multiplication on V by (A, w)(B, v) = (AB, wv). This is a polynomial map.
By Cramerâ€™s rule, the inverse is also a polynomial map.

GLn(K).

Other groups are interpretable in algebraically closed ï¬elds. For example,
let P2 be the projective plane over an algebraically closed ï¬eld, and let
E âŠ‚P2 be the elliptic curve {(X, Y, Z) âˆˆP2 : Y 2Z = X3 + XZ2}. The

then A âŠ•B âŠ•C = 0. By Theorem 3.2.20, (E, âŠ•, O) is an Ï‰-stable group.
In Section 7.4, we will give a general deï¬nition of algebraic groups that
includes both the linear algebraic groups and elliptic curves.
One of our main themes in this chapter will be that Ï‰-stable groups
behave very much like algebraic groups. Indeed, the following conjecture is
one of the guiding problems in the subject.
Cherlinâ€“Zilâ€™ber Conjecture If G is an inï¬nite simple group of ï¬nite
Morley rank, then G interprets an algebraically closed ï¬eld K and G is
deï¬nably isomorphic to a simple algebraic group deï¬ned over K.
Chain Conditions
If G is a group, we write H â‰¤G if H is a subgroup of G and H âŠ´G if H
is a normal subgroup of G.
In algebraically closed ï¬elds, there are no inï¬nite descending chains of
Zariski closed sets. Thus, in linear algebraic groups there are no inï¬nite de-
scending chains of algebraic subgroups. We will generalize this to arbitrary
Ï‰-stable groups.
Suppose that G is an Ï‰-stable group and H â‰¤G is a deï¬nable subgroup.
Because H âŠ†G, RM(H) â‰¤RM(G). For a âˆˆG \ H, the coset aH is a
subset of G disjoint from H and, because x 	â†’ax is a deï¬nable bijection,
RM(H) = RM(aH). Thus, if [G : H] is inï¬nite, then RM(H) < RM(G).
If 1 < [G : H] < â„µ0, then RM(H) = RM(G) but degM(H) < degM(G),
indeed, degM(G) = [G : H]degM(H). These easy observations have very
important consequences.
Theorem 7.1.2 (Descending Chain Condition) If G is an Ï‰-stable
group, then there is no inï¬nite descending chain of deï¬nable subgroups
G > G1 > G2 > . . . .
Proof

Ï‰-stable Fields
We will conclude this section by using generic types to prove two important
results. The ï¬rst is Macintyreâ€™s theorem that any inï¬nite Ï‰-stable ï¬eld is
algebraically closed. The second is Reinekeâ€™s theorem that minimal Ï‰-stable
groups are Abelian.
The proof of Macintyreâ€™s Theorem uses the following result from Galois
theory (see [58] VIII Â§6 Theorems 10 and 11).
Theorem 7.2.9 a) Suppose that L/K is a cyclic Galois extension of degree
n, where n is relatively prime to the characteristic of K and K contains
all nth roots of unity. The minimal polynomial of L/K is Xn âˆ’a for some
a âˆˆK.
b) Suppose that K has characteristic p > 0 and L/K is a Galois extension
of degree p. The minimal polynomial of L/K is Xp+X âˆ’a for some a âˆˆK.
Theorem 7.2.10 If (K, +, Â·, . . .) is an inï¬nite Ï‰-stable ï¬eld, then K is
algebraically closed.
Proof
We ï¬rst show that the additive group (K, +, . . .) is connected.
Suppose that K0 is the connected component of the additive group. For
a âˆˆK \ {0}, x 	â†’ax is a deï¬nable group automorphism. By Lemma 7.1.8
K0 is closed under multiplication by a. Thus, K0 is an ideal of K. Because
K is a ï¬eld, there are no proper ideals and K0 = K.
Because K is connected as an additive group, there is a unique type of
maximal Morley rank. Thus, the multiplicative group (KÃ—, Â·, . . .) is also
connected.
For each natural number n, the map x 	â†’xn is a multiplicative homo-
morphism. If a is generic, then, because an is interalgebraic with a, an is
also generic. Thus, Kn, the subgroup of nth powers, contains the generic.
Because the multiplicative group is connected, Kn = K and every element
has an nth root. In particular, if K has characteristic p > 0, then every
element of K has a pth root. Thus, K is perfect.
Suppose that K has characteristic p > 0. The map x 	â†’xp + x is an
additive homomorphism. If a is generic, then, because ap + a is interalge-
braic with a, ap + a is also generic. Thus, as above, the homomorphism is
surjective.
Claim 1 Suppose that K is an inï¬nite Ï‰-stable ï¬eld containing all mth
roots of unity for m â‰¤n. Then, K has no proper Galois extensions of
degree n.
Let n be least such that there is an Ï‰-stable ï¬eld K containing all mth
roots of unity for m â‰¤n and K has a proper Galois extension L of degree n.

Deï¬nition 7.3.1 We say that a deï¬nable X âŠ†G is indecomposable if and

Finding a Field
If G is an algebraic group over an algebraically closed ï¬eld K, then the ï¬eld
K is interpretable in G. Indeed, if V is any inï¬nite variety, we can ï¬nd a
projection map Ï€ : V â†’K such that the image of V is a coï¬nite subset of

7. Ï‰-Stable Groups
K. We can use the equivalence relation x âˆ¼y if and only if Ï€(x) = Ï€(y) to

To have any hope of proving the Cherlinâ€“Zilâ€™ber Conjecture, we would

algebraically closed ï¬eld. For the remainder of this section, we will show
how the Indecomposability Theorem gives us ways to interpret ï¬elds in
some ï¬nite Morley rank groups.

7.4
Deï¬nable Groups in Algebraically Closed
Fields
In this section, we will investigate groups interpretable in algebraically
closed ï¬elds. Our goal is to show that any such group is deï¬nably isomor-
phic to an algebraic group. If G is interpretable in an algebraically closed
ï¬eld K, then, by elimination of imaginaries, there is a deï¬nable X âŠ†Kn
and a deï¬nable f : X Ã— X â†’X such that (G, Â·) is deï¬nably isomorphic

to (X, f). Thus, to study interpretable groups it suï¬ƒces to study groups
where the underlying set and multiplication are deï¬nable sets. In alge-
braically closed ï¬elds, the deï¬nable subsets are exactly the constructible
subsets, so our goal is to show that any constructible group is deï¬nably
isomorphic to an algebraic group.

Algebraic Groups
Deï¬nition 7.4.8 An algebraic group is a group (G, Â·) where G is a variety
and Â· and x 	â†’xâˆ’1 are morphisms.
We derive some basic properties of algebraic groups.
Lemma 7.4.9 A deï¬nable subgroup of an algebraic group is closed.
Proof Suppose that G is an algebraic group and H â‰¤G is deï¬nable. Let
V be the closure of H in G. Suppose, for contradiction, that a âˆˆV \ H.
By Exercise 6.6.14, RM(V \ H) < RM(H). Every open set containing a
intersects H. If b âˆˆH, then x 	â†’bx is continuous. Thus, every open set

containing ba intersects H and Ha âŠ†V \ H. But x 	â†’xa is a deï¬nable

a contradiction.
In particular an algebraic group is connected if it has no proper algebraic
subgroups of ï¬nite index.
Lemma 7.4.10 A connected algebraic group is irreducible.
Proof
Let G be a connected algebraic group. Let V1, . . . , Vm be the ir-
reducible components of G. If a âˆˆG, then x 	â†’ax is continuous. Thus,
each aVi is irreducible and G = aV1 âˆª. . . âˆªaVm. Because the decom-
position into irreducible components is unique, G acts on the irreducible
components. Because there are only ï¬nitely many irreducible components,
H = {a : aV1 = V1} is a ï¬nite index subgroup of G. Because G is con-
nected, we must have H = G. Because aV1 = V1 for all a âˆˆG, we must
have V1 = G.
For the remainder of this section, we will make the simplifying assump-
tion that K has characteristic zero so that we do not have to deal with
quasirational functions. In characteristic p > 0, our proof will show that
every constructible group is deï¬nably isomorphic to a â€œquasialgebraicâ€
group (i.e., one where multiplication and inversion are quasimorphisms).
A second argument is then needed to show that quasialgebraic groups are
deï¬nably isomorphic to algebraic groups. We refer the reader to [86] for
details in this case.
We will be doing some model-theoretic arguments with generic types
and generic elements of our ï¬eld. Let K â‰ºK be a monster model. The
formulas deï¬ning a group G in K will deï¬ne a group G in K that will also
be a monster model. If X âŠ†G is deï¬nable and a âˆˆG, we will sometimes
write â€œa âˆˆX,â€ to mean that a satisï¬es the formula deï¬ning X.

Deï¬nition 7.4.17 Suppose that k and l are diï¬€erential ï¬elds with k âŠ†l.
The diï¬€erential Galois group G(l/k) is the group of all diï¬€erential ï¬eld
automorphisms of l that ï¬x the ï¬eld k pointwise.
Diï¬€erential Galois groups were ï¬rst studied when l is obtained from k
by adjoining solutions of linear diï¬€erential equations. We say that f(X) âˆˆ
k{X} is a linear diï¬€erential polynomial over k if

where a0, . . . , an, b âˆˆk. If an Ì¸= 0, then we say that f has order n. If b = 0,
we say that f(X) = 0 is a homogeneous linear diï¬€erential equation.

The next lemma summarizes some of the basic facts we will need about
linear diï¬€erential equations. If k is a diï¬€erential ï¬eld, we let Ck denote the
constant ï¬eld of k.
Lemma 7.4.19 Let k be a diï¬€erential ï¬eld with constants Ck. Let f(X) âˆˆ
k{X} be a homogeneous linear diï¬€erential polynomial of order n.
i) The solutions to f(X) = 0 in k form a vector space over Ck of dimen-
sion at most n.
ii) Let x0, . . . , xn âˆˆk; then W(x0, . . . , xn) = 0 if and only if x0, . . . , xn
are linearly dependent over Ck.
iii) Let K âŠ‡k be diï¬€erentially closed. There are x1, . . . , xn âˆˆK solutions
to f(X) = 0 such that x1, . . . , xn are linearly independent over CK.
Proof
i) and ii) are standard facts that can be found in any book on
diï¬€erential equations (for example, [39] or [65]).
iii) Given x1, . . . , xm with m < n. Because W(x1, . . . , xm+1) has or-
der m < n, we can ï¬nd xm+1 âˆˆK such that f(xm+1) = 0 and
W(x1, . . . , xm+1) Ì¸= 0.
In particular, if K is diï¬€erentially closed with constant ï¬eld CK, and
f(X) = 0 is a homogeneous linear diï¬€erential equation of order n, then
there are x1, . . . , xn linearly independent over CK such that the solution
set for f(X) = 0 is exactly the CK-vector space spanned by x1, . . . , xn. In
this case, we call x1, . . . , xn a fundamental system of solutions.
Deï¬nition 7.4.20 Let l/k be diï¬€erential ï¬elds. We say that l is a Picardâ€“
Vessiot extension of k if there is a homogeneous linear diï¬€erential equation
f(X) = 0 and x1, . . . , xn âˆˆl a fundamental system of solutions such that
l = kâŸ¨x1, . . . , xnâŸ©and Ck = Cl. We say that l/k is a Picardâ€“Vessiot exten-
sion for f.
Using diï¬€erential closures, it is easy to show the existence of Picardâ€“
Vessiot extensions. Recall that in Exercise 6.6.32 we proved that if k is
a diï¬€erential ï¬eld and K is the diï¬€erential closure of k, then CK is the
algebraic closure of Ck. In particular, if Ck is algebraically closed, then
CK = Ck.

The Group Conï¬guration
Theorem 7.5.4 is the ï¬rst example of a theorem where we produce a deï¬n-
able group once we have spotted some trace evidence of its existence. We
conclude this section by stating a version of the most powerful result of
this type.
Deï¬nition 7.5.6 Suppose that M is Ï‰-stable. We call a, b, c, x, y, z âˆˆMeq
a group conï¬guration if:
i) RM(a) = RM(b) = RM(c) = RM(x) = RM(y) = RM(z) = 1;
ii) any pair of elements has rank 2;
iii) RM(a, b, c) = RM(c, x, y) = RM(a, y, z) = RM(b, x, z) = 2;
iv) any other triple has rank 3;
v) RM(a, b, c, x, y, z) has rank 3.
We represent the group conï¬guration by the following diagram.

x
The points in the diagram have rank 1. Conditions iii) and iv) assert
that each line has rank 2 while any three non-collinear points have rank 3.
There is one easy way that a group conï¬guration arises. Suppose that
G is a strongly minimal Abelian group. Let a, b, x be independent generic
elements of G. Let c = ba, y = cx, and z = bx; then, y = az and it is easy
to check that conditions i)â€“v) hold. Remarkably, Hrushovski proved that
whenever there is a group conï¬guration there is also a deï¬nable group.
Theorem 7.5.7 Suppose that there is a group conï¬guration in Meq. Then,
there is a rank one group deï¬nable in Meq.
We give an application of the group conï¬guration in Theorem 8.3.1.

Remarks
We know some things about the Cherlinâ€“Zilâ€™ber Conjecture for groups of
very small rank. Of course, a connected group of rank 1 is Abelian. Cherlin
proved that there are no non-Abelian simple groups of rank 2.
Theorem 7.6.24 If G is a connected rank 2 group, then G is solvable.
Problems arise in the analysis starting at rank 3.
Deï¬nition 7.6.25 We say that G is a bad group if G is connected, non-
solvable, and all proper connected deï¬nable subgroups of G are nilpotent.
An algebraic group over an algebraically closed ï¬eld is not a bad group.
The real algebraic group SO3(R) is connected, nonsolvable, and all real
algebraic subgroups are one-dimensional, but SO3(R) is unstable. It is not
known whether there are any bad groups of ï¬nite Morley rank. The next
result of Cherlin shows how bad groups could be obstacles to proving the
Cherlinâ€“Zilâ€™ber Conjecture.
Theorem 7.6.26 If G is a simple group of Morley rank 3, then
i) G is a bad group, or
ii) G interprets an algebraically closed ï¬eld K and G is deï¬nably iso-
morphic to PSL2(K).
For proofs of these results and more on groups of ï¬nite Morley rank, see
[86] or [15].

288
7. Ï‰-Stable Groups
Macintyre ï¬rst noted the Descending Chain Condition for Ï‰-stable
groups. Although the Descending Chain Condition does not hold for sta-
ble groups, Baldwin and Saxl showed that there are no uniformly deï¬ned
descending chains (see Exercise 7.6.1).
The notion of generic types is due to Poizat. There are important gen-
eralizations in stable and superstable groups (see [86]).
The proof we gave that an Ï‰-stable ï¬eld is algebraically closed follows
the proof given by Cherlin and Shelah, who, using U-rank, generalized the
result to superstable ï¬elds.
The Indecomposability Theorem and its consequences for groups of ï¬nite
Morley rank are due to Zilâ€™ber. The exercises on ï¬nite Morley rank ï¬elds
are due to Poizat and Wagner [101].
Theorem 7.4.14 was ï¬rst stated by van den Dries, who noticed that
it follows from Weilâ€™s group chunk theorem. The proof above was given
by Hrushovski. In fact, one can prove a stronger version of the theorem.
Suppose that the constructible group G is deï¬ned over a subï¬eld k; then G
is deï¬nably isomorphic to an algebraic group deï¬ned over k. This requires
a descent argument of Weilâ€™s. A sketch of the proof is given in [77].
The model theoretic treatment of Kolchinâ€™s diï¬€erential Galois theory
is due to Poizat. Surveys of diï¬€erential Galois theory for linear diï¬€eren-
tial equations can be found in [50], [63], and [96]. Pillay [78] developed a
generalized diï¬€erential Galois theory where arbitrary ï¬nite Morley rank
diï¬€erential algebraic groups arise as Galois groups.

Geometry of Strongly Minimal Sets
8.1
Pregeometries
In our proof of Morleyâ€™s Categoricity Theorem in Chapter 6, we examined
the algebraic closure relation on strongly minimal sets. In this chapter, we
will return to strongly minimal sets and study more carefully the combi-
natorial geometry of algebraic closure. One of the great insights of modern
model theory is that the local properties of the geometry of strongly min-
imal sets have a great inï¬‚uence on global properties of structures. These
ideas play an important role in Hrushovskiâ€™s proof of the Mordellâ€“Lang
Conjecture for function ï¬elds. In Theorem 8.3.20, we sketch how this works
in one simple case.
We begin by reviewing some basic ideas from combinatorial geometry.
The proofs are quite easy, and we leave them as exercises.
Deï¬nition 8.1.1 Let X be a set and let cl : P(X) â†’P(X) be an operator
on the power set of X. We say that (X, cl) is a pregeometry if the following
conditions are satisï¬ed.
i) If A âŠ†X, then A âŠ†cl(A) and cl(cl(A)) = cl(A).
ii) If A âŠ†B âŠ†X, then cl(A) âŠ†cl(B).
iii) (exchange) If A âŠ†X, a, b âˆˆX, and a âˆˆcl(Aâˆª{b}), then a âˆˆcl(A) or
b âˆˆcl(A âˆª{a}).
iv) (ï¬nite character) If A âŠ†X and a âˆˆcl(A), then there is a ï¬nite
A0 âŠ†A such that a âˆˆcl(A0).
We say that A âŠ†X is closed if cl(A) = A.

8.1 Pregeometries

Example 8.1.8 Pure Sets
Let D be a set with no structure. Then for all a âˆˆD, acl(a) = {a} and
acl(âˆ…) = âˆ…. Thus, (D, acl) is a trivial geometry.
Example 8.1.9 Successor
Let D |= Th(Z, s), where s(x) = x + 1. Then, acl(âˆ…) = âˆ…and acl(A) =
{sn(a) : a âˆˆA, n âˆˆZ} for any A âŠ†D. Thus, (D, acl) is a trivial pregeom-

Example 8.1.10 Projective Geometry
Let F be a division ring and V be an inï¬nite vector space over F. We view
V as a structure in the language L = {+, 0, Î»a : a âˆˆF} where Î»a(x) = ax.
Then, V is a strongly minimal set, and for any set A âŠ†V the algebraic
closure of A is equal to the smallest F-subspace spanned by A. The usual
dimension theorem for intersections of linear subspaces shows that this
pregeometry is modular. This is not a geometry because cl(âˆ…) = {0} and
for any a âˆˆV \{0}, cl(a) is the line through a and 0. To form the associated
geometry, we take as points the lines through 0. The closure of a set of lines
is the set of all lines in their linear span. Thus, the associated geometry is
just the projective space associated to V . If dim V = n, then the projective
space has dimension n âˆ’1.
Example 8.1.11 Aï¬ƒne Geometry
Let V and F be as above. We deï¬ne a second geometry on V where
the closure of a set A is the smallest aï¬ƒne space containing it and
cl(âˆ…) = âˆ…. (An aï¬ƒne space is any translate of a linear space). Here
cl({a}) = {a}, so this is a geometry. Let a, b, c âˆˆV be noncollinear. Then,
dim(a, b, c, c + b âˆ’a) = 3, whereas dim(a, b) = dim(c, c + b âˆ’a) = 2 and
cl(a, b) âˆ©cl(c, c + b âˆ’a) = âˆ…because these are parallel lines. Thus, the
geometry is not modular. If we localize at zero, then the pregeometry is
exactly projective geometry, so this is locally modular.
For F = Q, we can view this as the algebraic closure geometry of a
strongly minimal set by viewing V as a structure in the language {Ï„}
where Ï„(x, y, z) = x + y âˆ’z. (For arbitrary F, add function symbols for
ax + y âˆ’az for each a âˆˆF.)
Example 8.1.12 Algebraically Closed Fields

independent over k. Let y = ax + b. Then, dim(k(x, y, a, b)/k) = 3 and

Next, we give a useful characterization of modularity.
Lemma 8.1.13 Let (X, cl) be a pregeometry. The following are equivalent.

One-Based Theories
We give one further characterization of locally modular strongly minimal
sets. In Exercise 8.4.6, we show that a pregeometry is modular if and only

algebraically closed in Meq are independent (in the sense of forking) over
A âˆ©B.

B = acleq(B), then A |âŒ£Aâˆ©BB.

8.3
Geometry and Algebra
In this section, we will sketch some important results showing the relation-
ship between the geometry of strongly minimal sets and the presence of
deï¬nable algebraic structure. We conclude with a sketch of how these ideas
come together in Hrushovskiâ€™s proof of the Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture for
function ï¬elds.
Nontrivial Locally Modular Strongly Minimal Sets
So far, the only examples we have given of nontrivial locally modular
strongly minimal sets are aï¬ƒne and projective geometries. In both cases,
there is a group present. The following remarkable theorem of Hrushovski
shows that this is always the case.
Theorem 8.3.1 Suppose that M is strongly minimal, nontrivial, and lo-
cally modular; then there is an inï¬nite group deï¬nable in Meq.
Proof We will deduce this result using the group conï¬guration theorem.
A direct proof of this result would be an easier special case of the group
conï¬guration theorem. The reader can ï¬nd direct proofs in [18] or [76].
Because M is nontrivial, we can ï¬nd a ï¬nite A âŠ‚M and b, c âˆˆM\acl(A)
such that c âˆˆacl(A, b) \ (acl(A) âˆªacl(b)). Choose d âˆˆM independent from
A, b, c. By Theorem 8.2.11, Md is modular. Adding d to the language, we
may assume that M is modular. Because d is independent from A, b, c, we
still have c âˆˆacl(A, b) \ (acl(A) âˆªacl(b)).
Let C = acl(A) âˆ©acl(b, c). By modularity (see Exercise 8.4.6),
dim(b, c, A) = dim(b, c) + dim(A) âˆ’dim C.
Because dim(b, c, A) = dim A+1 and dim(b, c) = 2, dim C = 1. Thus, there
is a âˆˆC with dim(a) = 1. Note that
dim(a, c) = dim(a, b) = dim(b, c) = dim(a, b, c) = 2.
Choose y, z âˆˆM such that (b, c) and (y, z) realize the same type over
acleq(a) and (y, z) are independent from (b, c) over a (i.e., dim(y, z/a, b, c) =

8.3 Geometry and Algebra
301
dim(y, z/a) = dim(b, c/a) = 1). Thus, dim(a, b, c, x, y) = 3. Because (y, z)
and (b, c) realize the same type over a,
dim(a, z) = dim(a, y) = dim(y, z) = dim(a, y, z) = 2.
Because a âˆˆacl(b, c), z âˆˆacl(b, c, y). Thus, dim(b, c, y) = 3 and dim(b, y) =
2. Symmetric arguments show that
dim(b, z) = dim(c, y) = dim(c, z) = 2
and
dim(b, c, z) = dim(b, y, z) = dim(c, y, z) = 3.
Let X = acl(c, y) âˆ©acl(b, z). By modularity,
dim(c, y, b, z) = dim(c, y) + dim(b, z) âˆ’dim X.
Thus, dim X = 1 and there is x âˆˆX with dim(x) = 1. Then
dim(c, x, y) = dim(c, x) = dim(c, y) = 2
and
dim(b, x, z) = dim(b, x) = dim(b, z) = 2.
If u = y or z and v = b or c, then a, b, c, x, y, z âˆˆacl(a, u, v), thus;
dim(a, u, v) = 3. Similarly, dim(b, x, y) = dim(c, x, z) = 3.
Using the fact that dimension and Morley rank are the same in strongly
minimal sets, we have:
i) RM(a) = RM(b) = RM(c) = RM(x) = RM(y) = RM(z) = 1;
ii) any pair of elements has rank 2;
iii) RM(a, b, c) = RM(c, x, y) = RM(a, y, z) = RM(b, x, z) = 2;
iv) all other triples have rank 3;
v) RM(a, b, c, x, y, z) = 3.
Thus, a, b, c, x, y, z is a group conï¬guration and, by Theorem 7.5.7, there
is a deï¬nable rank one group in Meq.
Theorem 8.3.1 is just the beginning of the story. In fact, the group that
we deï¬ne tells us a great deal about the structure of M. For example,
Hrushovski proved the following.
Theorem 8.3.2 Let M be a nontrivial locally modular strongly minimal
set. Then, there is a rank 1 Abelian group G deï¬nable in Meq such that G
acts deï¬nably as a group of automorphisms of the unique rank 1 type of M.

Zariski Geometries
In Theorem 8.3.1, we saw that nontrivial locally modular, strongly min-
imal sets interpret groups. Zilâ€™ber conjectured that if a strongly mini-
mal set is nonlocally modular, it is because it interprets an algebraically
closed ï¬eld. As we noted above, Hrushovski refuted Zilâ€™berâ€™s conjecture,
but Hrushovski and Zilâ€™ber found an important class of strongly minimal
sets where Zilâ€™berâ€™s conjecture is true. This work also answers the inter-
esting metamathematical question: Can one characterize the topological
spaces that arise from the Zariski topology on an algebraic curve?2 We will
describe their work but refer the reader to [44] and [45] for the proofs and
[67] for a more lengthy survey.
We say that a topological space is Noetherian if there are no inï¬nite
descending chains of closed sets. If K is a ï¬eld, then the Zariski topology
on Kn is Noetherian. If K is a diï¬€erential ï¬eld, the Kolchin topology on
Kn is Noetherian (see Exercise 4.5.43).
A closed set X is irreducible if there are no proper closed subsets X0 and
X1 such that X = X0 âˆªX1. The following lemma is an easy application of
KÂ¨onigâ€™s Lemma, we leave the proof as an exercise.
Lemma 8.3.10 Suppose that X is a Noetherian topology. If Y âŠ†X is
closed, then there are irreducible closed sets Y1, . . . , Yn such that Y
=
Y1 âˆª. . . âˆªYn and Y is not the union of any proper subset of {Y1, . . . , Yn}.
Moreover, if W1, . . . , Wm are irreducible closed sets, Y = W1 âˆª. . . âˆªWm,
and Y is not the union of any proper subset of {W1, . . . , Wm}, then n = m
and W1, . . . , Wn is a permutation of Y1, . . . , Yn. We call Y1, . . . , Yn the ir-
reducible components of Y .
2This work could be thought of in the same spirit as the result that a Pappian
projective plane is coordinatized by a ï¬eld.

8. Geometry of Strongly Minimal Sets
In Noetherian topological spaces, we can inductively assign an ordinal
dimension to any nonempty irreducible closed set X by
dim X = sup{dim Y + 1 : Y âŠ‚X, Y Ì¸= âˆ…, Y irreducible}.
Note that if a âˆˆX, then dim{a} = sup âˆ…= 0. The dimension of a reducible
closed set is the maximum dimension of an irreducible component.
Deï¬nition 8.3.11 A Zariski geometry is an inï¬nite set D and a sequence
of Noetherian topologies on D, D2, D3, . . . such that the following axioms
hold.
(Z0) i) If f : Dn â†’Dm is deï¬ned by f(x) = (f1(x), . . . , fm(x)) where
each fi : Dn â†’D is either constant or a coordinate projection, then f is
continuous.
ii) Each diagonal âˆ†n
i,j = {x âˆˆDn : xi = xj} is closed.
(Z1) (Weak QE): If C âŠ†Dn is closed and irreducible, and Ï€ : Dn â†’Dm
is a projection, then there is a closed F âŠ‚Ï€(C) such that Ï€(C) âŠ‡Ï€(C)\F.
(Z2) (Uniform one-dimensionality): i) D is irreducible.
ii) Let C âŠ†Dn Ã— D be closed and irreducible. For a âˆˆDn, let C(a) =
{x âˆˆD : (a, x) âˆˆC}. There is a number N such that, for all a âˆˆDn, either
|C(a)| â‰¤N or C(a) = D. In particular, any proper closed subset of D is

(Z3) (Dimension theorem): Let C âŠ†Dn be closed and irreducible. Let W
be a nonempty irreducible component of Câˆ©âˆ†n
i,j. Then dim C â‰¤dim W +1.
The basic example of a Zariski geometry is a smooth algebraic curve C
over an algebraically closed ï¬eld where Cn is equipped with the Zariski
topology. In this case, Z0 is clear, Z1 follows from quantiï¬er elimination,
and Z2 follows from the fact that C is strongly minimal. The veriï¬cation
of Z3 uses the smoothness of C (although a weaker condition suï¬ƒces).
Zariski geometries may be locally modular. If X is an inï¬nite set, we can
topologize Xn by taking the positive quantiï¬er-free deï¬nable sets in the
language of equality (allowing parameters) as the closed sets. This deter-
mines a trivial Zariski geometry on X. If K is a ï¬eld, we could topologize
Kn by taking the aï¬ƒne subsets (i.e., cosets of subspaces). This is a non-
trivial, locally modular Zariski geometry. Clearly, a locally modular Zariski
geometry will not interpret a ï¬eld, but Hrushovski and Zilâ€™ber showed that
this is the only restriction.
First, one must see how model theory enters the picture. Given a Zariski
geometry D, let L be the language with an n-ary relation symbol for each
closed subset of Dn. Let D be D viewed in the natural way as an L-
structure.
Lemma 8.3.12 The theory of D admits quantiï¬er elimination, and D is
a strongly minimal set. Moreover, Morley rank in D is exactly dimension.

Hrushovski and Zilâ€™ber give a more reï¬ned version of Theorem 8.3.13

Deï¬nition 8.3.14 We say that a family of plane curves is ample if when-
ever x and y are independent generic points of D2 there is a plane curve
Ca with x, y âˆˆCa.
An ample family is called very ample if for x, y (not necessarily inde-

y Ì¸âˆˆC(a). In this case, we say that the family separates points.
We say that D is (very) ample if there is a (very) ample family of plane
curves.
Using Theorem 8.2.11 it is not hard to show that a Zariski geometry is
nonlocally modular if and only if it is ample.
Corollary 8.3.15 If D is an ample Zariski geometry, then D interprets
an algebraically closed ï¬eld.
For most model-theoretic applications, we only need the results for ample
Zariski geometries, but the following result shows that very ample geome-
tries are intimately related to smooth algebraic curves.
Theorem 8.3.16 If D is a very ample Zariski geometry, then there is an
interpretable algebraically closed ï¬eld K, C a smooth quasiprojective curve
deï¬ned over K, and a deï¬nable bijection f : D â†’C such that the induced
maps f n : Dn â†’Cn are homeomorphisms for all n.

in diï¬€erentially closed ï¬elds (see [67] for a proof).
Theorem 8.3.17 If K is a diï¬€erentially closed ï¬eld and D is a strongly
minimal set, there is a ï¬nite X âŠ‚D such that if we topologize (D\X)n with

This shows that, in diï¬€erentially closed ï¬elds, any nonlocally modular,

Applications to Diophantine Geometry
Theorem 8.3.9 plays an important role in Hrushovskiâ€™s applications of
model theory to Diophantine geometry. We will try to brieï¬‚y give the

Deï¬nition 8.3.18
Suppose that K is an algebraically closed ï¬eld. An
algebraic group A that is embedded as an irreducible closed set in projective
n-space over K is called an Abelian variety. We say that A is simple if A
has no proper inï¬nite algebraic subgroups.
For example, an elliptic curve is a simple Abelian variety. The next lemma
summarizes some of the group-theoretic properties of Abelian varieties. See
[38] for details.
Lemma 8.3.19 Suppose that A is an Abelian variety.
i) A is a commutative divisible group.
ii) If K has characteristic zero, the subgroup of n-torsion points of A has
size n2d, where d is the dimension of A.
iii) The torsion points of A are Zariski dense in A.
If G is an algebraic group and we consider the structure (G, Â·, . . .) where
we have predicates for all constructible subsets of Gn, then G is not one-
based. Indeed, we can always ï¬nd a deï¬nable subset X âŠ†G and a deï¬nable
map X 	â†’K such that the image is a coï¬nite subset of K. Using this map,
we can interpret the ï¬eld, a nonlocally modular, strongly minimal set,
contradicting Exercise 8.4.14.
Surprisingly, this is not true for diï¬€erential algebraic groups. Let K be
a diï¬€erentially closed ï¬eld, and let C be the ï¬eld of constants of K. If A
is an Abelian variety deï¬ned over K, then we can still view A as a group
deï¬nable in the diï¬€erentially closed ï¬eld K. In Exercise 4.5.43, we intro-
duced the Kolchin topology, the extension of the Zariski topology where we
also consider solution sets of algebraic diï¬€erential equations, and showed
that there are no inï¬nite descending chains of Kolchin closed sets. Even if
there are no interesting algebraic subgroups of A, there will be new Kolchin
closed subgroups. The following result is due to Hrushovski and SokoloviÂ´c,
drawing on earlier work of [64] and Buium[20].
Theorem 8.3.20 Suppose that K is a diï¬€erentially closed ï¬eld and A is a
simple Abelian variety deï¬ned over K that is not isomorphic to an Abelian
variety deï¬ned over C. Let A# be the closure in the Kolchin topology of

the torsion points of A. Then, A# is a one-based group and there are no
proper inï¬nite deï¬nable subgroups of A#.
The results of Manin and Buium show that A# is a ï¬nite Morley rank
group with no inï¬nite deï¬nable subgroups. There are two cases to consider.
If all strongly minimal subsets of A# are locally modular, then A# is one-

The following simple application is a special case of Hrushovskiâ€™s proof

Remarks
Zilâ€™ber was the ï¬rst to recognize the importance of studying the combina-
torial geometry of algebraic closure. Pillayâ€™s Geometric Stability Theory is
an excellent reference for the material in this chapter.
Although Zilâ€™berâ€™s conjecture is false, the analog for o-minimal theories
is true. Peterzil and Starchenko [74] proved that, in an o-minimal theory, if
there are two-dimensional families of plane curves, then one can interpret
a real closed ï¬eld.
Zariski geometries also arise when studying compact complex manifolds.
Suppose that M is a complex manifold. We say that X âŠ†M is analytic
if for all a âˆˆM there is an open neighborhood U of a and f1, . . . , fm
holomorphic functions on U such that
X âˆ©U = {x âˆˆU : f1(x) = . . . = fm(x) = 0}.
If M is compact, then there are no inï¬nite descending chains of analytic
subsets. Thus, we can give M n a Noetherian topology where the closed sets
are exactly the analytic subsets.
If M is a complex manifold we consider the structure M, where the
underlying set is M and we have relation symbols for each analytic subset
of M, M 2, M 3, . . .. If a âˆˆM, then {a} is analytic so this language will have
size 2â„µ0.
Zilâ€™ber proved the following result (see [80]).
Theorem 8.4.17 If M is a compact complex manifold, then M has elim-
ination of quantiï¬ers and is totally transcendental. Indeed, if A âŠ†M n is
analytic, then RM(A) is at most the complex dimension of A.
If M is strongly minimal, then M is a Zariski geometry.
One way to get a compact complex manifold is by taking a smooth pro-
jective variety. By Chowâ€™s Theorem (see [37] B.2.2), every analytic subset
of projective space is already deï¬ned algebraically. Thus, these examples
are interpretable in the ï¬eld C and hence nothing new. In particular, a
smooth projective variety is strongly minimal if and only if it is an alge-
braic curve. Riemannâ€™s Existence Theorem (see [37] B.3.1) says that any
one-dimensional compact complex manifold is a smooth projective alge-
braic curve. Thus, to look for new examples, we need to consider manifolds
of dimension greater than one.

8. Geometry of Strongly Minimal Sets
Suppose that Î±1, . . . , Î±2n âˆˆCn and Î±1, . . . , Î±2n are linearly independent
over R. Let Î› be the lattice ZÎ±1 âŠ•. . . âŠ•ZÎ±2n. We can form a compact
complex manifold M, by taking the quotient structure Cn/Î›. We call M a
complex torus. Because Î› is an additive subgroup of Cn, M is a complex
Lie group.
If Î±1, . . . , Î±2n are algebraically independent over Q, we call M a generic
torus. Classical results from complex manifolds (see [91] VIII 1.4) imply
that M is strongly minimal. Pillay [81] showed that M is a locally modular
group.
The most striking recent result in model theory is Hrushovskiâ€™s [43] proof
of the Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture for function ï¬elds. We conclude our re-
marks by explaining the conjecture and stating Hrushovskiâ€™s result.
Let K be an algebraically closed ï¬eld of characteristic zero and A an
Abelian variety deï¬ned over K. Suppose that Î“ is a subgroup of A. We
say that Î“ is ï¬nite rank if there is a ï¬nitely generated subgroup Î“0 such
that Î“ âŠ†{g âˆˆA : ng âˆˆÎ“0 for some n = 1, 2, . . .}. For example, taking
Î“0 = {0}, the torsion subgroup of A is of ï¬nite rank.
Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture (characteristic zero) Suppose that K has
characteristic zero, A is an Abelian variety, Î“ is a ï¬nite rank subgroup
of A and X is a proper subvariety of A. Then, X âˆ©Î“ is a ï¬nite union of
cosets of subgroups of A.
Next we show how the Mordell-Lang conjecture implies the Mordell Con-
jecture. Suppose that C is a curve of genus g > 1 deï¬ned over a number ï¬eld
k. The Mordell Conjecture asserts that C has only ï¬nitely many k-rational
points (i.e., points with coordinates in k).
To any curve C of genus g â‰¥1, we can associate a g-dimensional Abelian
variety J(C) deï¬ned over k called the Jacobian of C (see [38]). The curve C
is a subvariety of J(C), and J(C) is the smallest Abelian variety in which
C embeds. If C has genus 1, then C is an elliptic curve and J(C) = C.
Let C have genus g > 1. Let Î“ be the k-rational points of J(C). The
Mordellâ€“Weil theorem (see [38]) asserts that Î“ is a ï¬nitely generated group.
Thus, C âˆ©Î“ is a ï¬nite union of cosets of subgroups of Î“. If any of these
subgroups is inï¬nite, then the Zariski closure of that coset is also a coset
and the Zariski closure must be the entire curve C. But then there would
be a group structure deï¬ned on C and C would be an Abelian variety
contradicting the fact that J(C) âŠƒC and J(C) is the smallest Abelian
variety in which C embeds. Thus, C âˆ©Î“ is ï¬nite and C contains only
ï¬nitely many k-rational points.
The Mordell Conjecture was proved by Faltings, who also proved the
Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture in case Î“ is ï¬nitely generated. The full charac-
teristic zero Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture was proved by McQuillen, building
on Faltingsâ€™ work as well as work of Raynaud, Hindry, and Vojta.
In number theory, when studying question about number ï¬elds, it is often
insightful to ask the same question about ï¬nitely generated extensions of

8.4 Exercises and Remarks
313
algebraically closed ï¬elds (we call these function ï¬elds). Long before Falt-
ingsâ€™ work, Manin proved the function ï¬eld case of the Mordell Conjecture.
Theorem 8.4.18 Let k be algebraically closed of characteristic zero, and
let K âŠƒk. Let C be a curve of genus g > 1 deï¬ned over K. Then, either
C has only ï¬nitely many K-rational points or C is isomorphic to a curve
deï¬ned over k.
So far, we have only considered characteristic zero. What about charac-
teristic p > 0? The obvious generalization of the Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture
to characteristic p is false. Suppose that C is a curve of genus g > 1 deï¬ned
over Fp and let J(C) be its Jacobian. Let Î± be a generic point of C. Let
Î“ be the group of Fp(Î±)-rational points of J(C). By the Langâ€“NÂ´eron The-
orem (the function ï¬eld version of the Mordellâ€“Weil Theorem (see [38])),
Î“ is ï¬nitely generated. If Ïƒ is the Frobenius automorphism x 	â†’xp, then
Ïƒn(Î±) âˆˆC for all n. Thus, C âˆ©Î“ is inï¬nite, but C is not a coset of a sub-
group. In this case, our curve C is deï¬ned over the prime ï¬eld. Abramovich
and Voloch [1] stated a plausible version of the Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture
for function ï¬elds in characteristic p and proved it in many important cases.
Their conjecture was proved by Hrushovski, who also gave a new proof for
the function ï¬eld case in characteristic zero.
Theorem 8.4.19 (Mordellâ€“Lang Conjecture for function ï¬elds)
Let k be an algebraically closed ï¬eld with K âŠƒk. Let A be an Abelian
variety deï¬ned over K, X a subvariety of A, and Î“ a ï¬nite rank subgroup
of A. Suppose that Xâˆ©Î“ is Zariski dense in X. Then, there is a sub-Abelian
variety A1 âŠ†A, an Abelian variety B deï¬ned over k, a surjective homo-
morphism g : A1 â†’B, and a subvariety X0 of B deï¬ned over k such that
gâˆ’1(X0) is a translate of X.
For more on Hrushovskiâ€™s result, see [16], [17], and [79].

Appendix A
Set Theory
In this Appendix, we will survey some of the elementary results from set
theory that we use in the text. We give very few proofs and refer the reader
to set theory texts such as [26], [47], or [57] for further details.
We will work in ZFC, Zermeloâ€“Fraenkel set theory with the Axiom of
Choice. The Axiom of Choice asserts that if (Ai : i âˆˆI) is a family of
nonempty sets, then there is a function f with domain I such that f(i) âˆˆAi
for all i âˆˆI.
Zornâ€™s Lemma and Well-Orderings
If X is a set and < is a binary relation on X, we say that (X, <) is a partial
order if (X, <) |= âˆ€x Â¬(x < x) and (X, <) |= âˆ€xâˆ€yâˆ€z ((x < y âˆ§y < z) â†’
x < z).
We say that (X, <) is a linear order if in addition
(X, <) |= âˆ€xâˆ€y (x < y âˆ¨x = y âˆ¨y < x).
If (X, <) is a partial order, then we say that C âŠ†X is a chain in X if C
is linearly ordered by <.
Theorem A.1 (Zornâ€™s Lemma) If (X, <) is a partial order such that
for every chain C âŠ†X there is x âˆˆX such that c â‰¤x for all c âˆˆC, then
there is y âˆˆX such that there is no z âˆˆX with z > x. In other words, if
every chain has an upper bound, then there is a maximal element of X.

Let G = Gal(L/R) be the Galois group of L/R. Let H be the 2-Sylow
subgroup of G.
Claim 2 G = H.
Let F be the ï¬xed ï¬eld of H. Then F/R must have odd degree. If F =
R(x), then the minimal polynomial of x over R has odd degree, but the
only irreducible polynomials of odd degree are linear. Thus, F = R and
G = H.
Let G1 = Gal(L/K). If G1 is nontrivial, then there is G2 a subgroup of
G1 of index 2. Let F be the ï¬xed ï¬eld of G2. Then, F/K has degree 2.
But by Claim 1, K has no extensions of degree 2. Thus, G1 is trivial and
L = K.
